Now is the time to Develop Windows Universal Apps!|Desktop|I&rsquo;d like to take a moment and time travel back to late 2010, when Windows Phone 7 officially launched. Microsoft developers everywhere were excited that they could reuse their existing skillset and begin to build native mobile applications. Not only were Microsoft developers happy, but so was Telerik. We had been working closely with Microsoft to deliver a CTP of our <a href="http://blogs.telerik.com/windowsphoneteam/posts/10-10-28/telerik-controls-for-windows-phone-7---first-official-ctp-release.aspx">Windows Phone control suite</a> right at launch and become the first vendor to enter this space.<p>By mid-2011, Microsoft released the first public beta of Windows 8 to developers which included another SDK specific to the platform. Windows 8 was publicly released in mid-2012 and was a milestone in the company&rsquo;s history as it was designed for touch. This meant Microsoft&rsquo;s OS was headed for the tablet for the very first time. This was another victory for Microsoft developers as they could now develop for tablets running Windows 8. Again, Telerik had been working with Microsoft to deliver our <a href="http://blogs.telerik.com/Windows8Team/posts/12-06-06/premiere-of-telerik-radcontrols-for-metro-at-teched-12-the-first-commercial-library-for-windows-8.aspx">Windows 8 control suite</a> right at launch and we were the first commercial library available on the market.</p> <p>Fast forward to //Build in April 2014, Microsoft acknowledged the convergence of Windows 8.1 and Windows Phone 8.1 and gave it the title of Windows Universal Apps. For the first time in history, you could now create applications that target the phone and tablet and share a single code base. It should come to no surprise that Telerik is yet again <a href="http://blogs.telerik.com/windowsphoneteam/posts/14-08-07/telerik-ui-for-windows-universal-debuting-with-a-powerful-chart-library"><strong>among the first vendors</strong></a> on the market to release a control suite for Windows Universal Apps. This shows our commitment over the years to Microsoft and, more importantly, to you as the developer. So now that we&rsquo;ve had our history lesson, let&rsquo;s go to class and learn more about Windows Universal Apps and how Telerik fits in.</p> <h2>Let&rsquo;s begin by Looking at the Structure of a Universal App</h2> <p>Before we jump in, Windows Universal Apps require <a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/br211384.aspx">Visual Studio 2013</a> Update 2 or later. Once installed, expand the &ldquo;Store Apps&rdquo; node and you will see a category called &ldquo;Universal Apps&rdquo;. Select &ldquo;Blank App&rdquo; and give it a name. A blank app simply creates a single-page app that uses the Windows Runtime and targets both Windows and Windows Phone. The solution explorer looks like the following as shown in <em>Figure 1</em>.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]1cf2ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]1cf2ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="01" alt="01" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]1cf2ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p>Note that there are actually three projects created upon launch:</p> <ol> <li>Windows 8.1 App</li> <li>Windows Phone 8.1 App</li> <li>Shared Project &ndash; This is a container for code that runs on both platforms. Everything in this project is automatically included in both projects and in their build output.</li> </ol> <p>If you look under references, then you will notice that both the Windows 8.1 and Windows Phone 8.1 have a reference to the <em>Shared</em> project. This is where the magic starts to happen. Notice that the <code>App.xaml</code> and <code>App.xaml.cs</code> is only included in the Shared project. By using preprocessor directives such as the following, we can isolate sections of code to a specific platform:</p> <pre><code>#if WINDOWS_PHONE_APP  //run Windows Phone specifc code #endif #if WINDOWS_APP  //run Windows specific code #endif</code></pre>Now that we&rsquo;ve examined the structure of an app, let&rsquo;s dive deeper and add some functionality.<br /> <h2>A Quick Windows Universal App Demo</h2> <p>If you examine the contents of the <code>MainPage.xaml</code> in each project, then the only difference you will see is how the background color is set in each project. Windows 8.1 defines it in a Grid, whereas Windows Phone 8.1 declares it in the Page. Either approach works, so for this demo, lets drag and drop the <code>MainPage.xaml</code> file to our shared project and delete it from our Windows Phone 8.1 project.</p> <p>Go ahead and open the <code>MainPage.xaml</code> file from the Shared Project folder and take a look at the Toolbox and you will notice that Microsoft has supplied several controls that work just like they did in Windows 8 and Windows Phone 8. For the purpose of this demo, we are going to drag and drop a <code>TextBlock</code> onto the <code>MainPage.xaml</code>, and provide text and a font size. We can see our changes as we switch between Windows 8.1 and Windows Phone 8.1 in the designer as shown in <em>Figure 2</em>:</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]49f3ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]49f3ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="02" alt="02" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]49f3ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br />This application will run successfully if deployed to the Windows 8 simulator or Windows Phone emulator since the <code>OnLaunched</code> event in the <code>App.xaml.cs</code> has the following code looking for the <code>MainPage</code> file:<br /> <br /> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">if</code> <code style="color: #000000;">(!rootFrame.Navigate(</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">typeof</code><code style="color: #000000;">(MainPage), e.Arguments))</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">{</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">throw</code> <code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">new</code> <code style="color: #000000;">Exception(</code><code style="color: blue;">"Failed to create initial page"</code><code style="color: #000000;">);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></div> </div> <br />Since both projects are using the Windows Runtime, you could even use some common classes previously only found in Windows 8 in the <code>MainPage.xaml.cs</code>. This would trigger an appropriate <code>MessageDialog</code> on either platform.<br /> <br /> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">public</code> <code style="color: #000000;">MainPage()</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">{</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">this</code><code style="color: #000000;">.InitializeComponent();</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">Loaded += MainPage_Loaded;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">async </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">void</code> <code style="color: #000000;">MainPage_Loaded(</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">object</code> <code style="color: #000000;">sender, RoutedEventArgs e)</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">{</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">var dialog = </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">new</code> <code style="color: #000000;">MessageDialog(</code><code style="color: blue;">"Telerik Rocks!"</code><code style="color: #000000;">);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">await dialog.ShowAsync();</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></div> </div> <br /> <h2>Cleaning up our Shared Project</h2> <p>While we have a working application, as you continue to build Windows Universal Apps, you should adhere to the structure provided below to organize your code better. Using the HubApp as a Template, my Shared project now looks like <em>Figure 3</em>:<br /> <br /> <br /> </p> <p>Here you can see we have the following folders:</p> <ul> <li>Assets &ndash; Contains Images, Video, etc. that we would like each project to have access to.</li> <li>Common &ndash; Is great for helper classes, process lifetime management, etc.</li> <li>DataModel &ndash; Contains your data model which is specifically useful if you are using the MVVM pattern.</li> <li>Strings &ndash; Store your .resw files to allow your application to be localized.</li> </ul> <h2>With a properly structured app, we are now ready to create an app!</h2> <p>But wait, something is missing. Where is my <a href="http://www.telerik.com/windows-universal-ui/chart">Chart</a>, <a href="http://www.telerik.com/windows-universal-ui/autocompletebox">AutoCompleteBox</a> and <a href="http://www.telerik.com/windows-universal-ui/rating">Rating</a> control? What about a <a href="http://www.telerik.com/windows-universal-ui/busyindicator">BusyIndicator</a> that supports various animations instead of the one supplied by MS, or even a BusyIndicator that allows me to create my own animations. How about a customizable <a href="http://www.telerik.com/windows-universal-ui/datepicker">Date</a> and <a href="http://www.telerik.com/windows-universal-ui/timepicker">Time Picker</a>?</p> <p>This is where Telerik fills in the gaps with <a href="https://www.telerik.com/download-trial-file?pid=RCWindowsUniversal&amp;lict=1">Telerik UI for Universal Windows Apps</a>.</p> <p>Upon downloading and extracting it, you will have the binaries to reference all the UI controls mentioned earlier in your Windows Universal App, Help Documentation and a complete sample application that simulates a sales dashboard as shown in <em>Figure 4</em>.</p> <br /> <br /> <br />
Using the Windows Phone 8 Emulator with Hybrid Mobile Apps and Telerik AppBuilder|Mobile|<p>The Windows Phone 8 platform is widely considered, at best, a strong third option in mobile app development and, at worst, a reasonable consideration. Most often an afterthought to iOS or Android development, Windows Phone has steadily seen a small increase in marketshare and deserves it&rsquo;s fair share of investment in terms of debugging and testing tools. When we test Cordova WP8 apps today, we are usually relying on webkit-based simulators that are tweaked to mimic mobile IE &ndash; which is why testing on real devices and native emulators is even more important for Windows Phone than on other platforms.</p> <p>As part of a series where we discuss how to get up and running with the iOS, Android, and Windows Phone native emulators for hybrid mobile development, today we are going to dig into the Windows Phone 8 emulator. Not only will we cover how to install and configure our emulator, but we&rsquo;ll also see how easily we can use it in conjunction with <a href="http://www.telerik.com/appbuilder">Telerik AppBuilder</a>.</p> <p>Let&rsquo;s get started!</p> <ul> <li><a href="http://developer.telerik.com/featured/using-windows-phone-8-emulator-hybrid-mobile-apps-telerik-appbuilder/#wp8sdk">Setting up Your Windows Phone 8 Emulator Environment</a></li> <li><a href="http://developer.telerik.com/featured/using-windows-phone-8-emulator-hybrid-mobile-apps-telerik-appbuilder/#appbuilder">Using the Windows Phone 8 Emulator with Telerik AppBuilder</a></li> </ul> <p>&nbsp;</p> <h2>Setting up Your Windows Phone 8 Emulator Environment</h2> <p>The Windows Phone 8 (WP8) emulator is part of the Windows Phone 8 SDK, which is a free download from Microsoft. The exact SDK installation we are going to focus on is available <a href="http://go.microsoft.com/fwlink/p/?LinkId=265772">here</a>. You may notice this is the 8.0 SDK and not the 8.1 SDK. At the time of this writing, the latest version of Cordova (3.5) does not offer official support for Windows Phone 8.1 apps. This should change shortly though.</p> <p>Before you jump ahead and install the WP8 SDK, I want to caution you that there are some additional requirements to run the WP8 emulator:</p> <ul> <li>You must be running Windows 8 Pro or Enterprise</li> <li>You must be on a 64-bit machine (x64)</li> <li>Your computer must be capable of running <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyper-V">Hyper-V</a></li> </ul> <p>Normally all three of these are not issues with modern computers, but it&rsquo;s worth verifying your system just to be sure!</p> <h3>The Hyper-V Catch-22</h3> <p>If you have been following along with the <a href="http://developer.telerik.com/featured/using-android-emulator-hybrid-mobile-apps-telerik-appbuilder/">Android emulator guide</a>, I made specific mention of <em>disabling</em> Hyper-V on Windows machines to allow you to take advantage of Intel HAXM and speed up your emulators. However, this presents a bit of a catch-22 in that you need Hyper-V enabled to use the Windows Phone 8 emulator. If you don&rsquo;t enable Hyper-V and try to run the WP8 emulator, you&rsquo;ll hit an error like this:</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]40f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]40f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="hyperv" alt="hyperv" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]40f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <p>Thankfully, <a href="http://www.hanselman.com/blog/SwitchEasilyBetweenVirtualBoxAndHyperVWithABCDEditBootEntryInWindows81.aspx">Scott Hanselman has a nice post</a> on how to most efficiently switch between these two environments.</p> <p>If you&rsquo;ve gotten this far, it&rsquo;s time to install the SDK you downloaded. Go ahead and get a cup of coffee (or three) while the installer runs :). After your installation is done, there isn&rsquo;t anything else you have to do to configure your emulators &ndash; the next step is to try them out in AppBuilder!</p> <h2>Using the Windows Phone 8 Emulator with Telerik AppBuilder</h2> <p>Telerik AppBuilder makes it very easy to develop your cross-platform hybrid app with cloud-based tools and build services. And testing? Aside from the built-in device simulator (which works great with Windows Phone 8 apps) we can also test our apps on the WP8 emulator of course.</p> <h3>Using the AppBuilder extension for Visual Studio?</h3> <p>If you are developing with the <a href="http://www.telerik.com/appbuilder/visual-studio-extension">AppBuilder extension for Visual Studio</a>, simply go to the <code>APPBUILDER</code> menu in Visual Studio and select the <code>Run Native Emulator</code> option.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]4cf6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]4cf6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="vsmenu" alt="vsmenu" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]4cf6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br />This will bring up the Native Emulator dialog where you can choose to run your app against the Windows Phone emulator &ndash; and then choose which of the four default devices you would like to use:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]58f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]58f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="runemulator" alt="runemulator" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]58f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> <h3>Using the AppBuilder Windows Client?</h3> <p>Starting up a native emulator using the <a href="http://www.telerik.com/appbuilder/windows-client">AppBuilder Windows client</a> is much the same as in Visual Studio. To access the Native Emulators dialog, simply go to the <code>Run</code> menu and choose <code>Native Emulator</code>:</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]64f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]64f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="windowsclient" alt="windowsclient" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]64f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <h3>Launch Emulator</h3> <p>Regardless of which AppBuilder client you are using, the next step is the same. Simply click on the <code>Launch Emulator</code> button to bring up the emulator of your choice. The selected emulator will boot up in a few seconds. You can then use the <code>Build and Deploy</code> command to deploy your app to the emulator &ndash; exactly as if it were a physical device! (Note: An upcoming version of AppBuilder will handle the <code>Build and Deploy</code> step for you after you launch the emulator.)</p> <h4>The End Result</h4> <p>When you run your hybrid mobile app in the WP8 emulator in AppBuilder, you not only get to see how your app looks and responds on a WP8 device, but you also get to take advantage of LiveSync. This technology allows you to view any changes made in your app in real time on any physical device or native emulator. Now you don&rsquo;t have to re-build your app every time you make a change. Just tweak some code and the change is reflected instantly on the device!</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]70f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]70f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="friendsapp" alt="friendsapp" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]70f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <h2>What&rsquo;s Next?</h2> <p>The final part of this series will focus on using Xcode&rsquo;s iOS Simulator &ndash; which you&rsquo;ll be able to access from both Mac and Windows machines</p> <p><em>Header image courtesy of <a href="https://flic.kr/p/nTfZLC">Vernon Chan</a></em></p>
Why Developers Should Help With User Interface Tests|Testing|<p>Automated User Interface (UI) testing has a deserved reputation for being a morass of low-value, high-cost pain. Developers are already over-burdened trying to get the work in front of them completed and into the delivery pipeline. Why should they look to take on additional work in helping create functional tests at the User Interface level?</p> <h2>Ship Better Value</h2> <p>Every developer should care about delivering better value to their customers, regardless whether their customers are internal or external. Better value means the delivered software is in alignment with what the customer&rsquo;s true needs are (which is often not exactly what they asked for). Better value means software that is performant enough in the customer&rsquo;s environment to keep the customer&rsquo;s users happy. Better value means few, if any, bugs escaped out to the customer requiring support and patches.</p> <p>Important sounding stuff, no? And these are just a few of the reasons why developers ought to take interest and have a stake in UI-level testing.</p> <h2>Test the Scary Things</h2> <p>UI testing is time consuming, both for the initial creation and ongoing maintenance. With that in mind, teams that are already busy building software can&rsquo;t afford to disrupt their current flow for low-value, senseless automation.</p> <p>Instead, teams should focus on the most critical, high-value features. What&rsquo;s keeping your stakeholders awake at night? Generally it&rsquo;s around two broad areas:</p> <ul> <li>Can we make money?</li> <li>Are we going to get sued?</li> </ul> <p>Flippant prose aside, these really are two solid areas to focus on. Are the features that impact revenue (or your business-critical work) stable and working? Are you properly securing highly sensitive data? Of course you&rsquo;d use those as a starting base, but the point is to talk with your stakeholders and understand the most critical areas of the system.</p> <p>Take a shopping cart system as an example. Simple testing of the checkout system&rsquo;s UI isn&rsquo;t enough. You&rsquo;d want to ensure that any integrations with third-party payment providers such as PayPal, Discover and Visa are rock-solid. If integrations with those providers aren&rsquo;t working properly, then your company is likely losing revenue and incurring extra customer service costs due to failed transactions.</p> <p>That&rsquo;s the place where you begin building your UI scripts, not automating checks around look and feel or icon placement.</p> <h2>How Much to Test?</h2> <p>UI tests should be viewed as an extension and companion to other types of testing you are (or should be) doing already. A well-tested system will have a mix of different test types. Lisa Crispin and Janet Gregory use a &ldquo;testing pyramid&rdquo; as an example in their book <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Agile-Testing-Practical-Guide-Testers/dp/0321534468/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1400267262&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=lisa+crispin">Agile Testing</a>.</p> <p>Unit tests&ndash;those with no dependencies&ndash;should make up the majority of automated tests in a system because they&rsquo;re fast to write and run, and maintenance costs are lowest. The middle of the pyramid is integration tests&ndash;those crossing service boundaries&ndash;since they&rsquo;re slower to run and costlier to write and maintain. UI tests with their high costs should be at the top of the pyramid: a few carefully chosen tests that exercise high-value flows.</p> <p>Let&rsquo;s take some examples from computing wages as part of a payroll system. Here&rsquo;s how things might break out:</p> <ul> <li><strong>Unit Tests</strong>: Test the method/algorithm calculating wages. Validate proper wage is computed for workers based on inputs of hours worked, worker&rsquo;s hourly rate, and whether or not the worker earns overtime.</li> <li><strong>Integration Tests</strong>: Test interaction to other systems/layers. Check the security system integration to ensure only permitted users can execute payroll activities.</li> <li><strong>UI Tests</strong>: Check the flow of generating payroll. Validate an authorized user can click the right buttons and see that the payroll was properly executed.</li> </ul> <p>These last paragraphs centered on automated tests. You should be having some form of manual testing going on as well! I won&rsquo;t get into the debates around the various schools of testing. Suffice it to say, you should have skilled testers exploring your system alongside your automated tests.</p> <h2>Does It Really Work Like You Thought?</h2> <p>Interactions across boundaries are a frequent source of bugs. It&rsquo;s not surprising because invoking those calls, managing their lifecycle, and handling errors/exceptions is a relatively difficult task. Integration tests help drive great design and solid API behavior across those boundaries, but it&rsquo;s not enough if you stop there.</p> <p>Testing a workflow from the UI all the way down to the database is the final step in ensuring your systems&rsquo; interactions are behaving properly. Is your UI updating properly after a CRUD action? An integration or unit test wouldn&rsquo;t have caught that. Is caching interfering with updating rows on your grid? Whoops! Discovering that is the domain of a functional UI test too.</p> <h2>Automation as Acceptance Tests</h2> <p>Automation frameworks can also serve as acceptance criteria for new work, regardless of whether you&rsquo;re using some form of specification toolset (See Gojko Adzik&rsquo;s wonderful book <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Specification-Example-Successful-Deliver-Software/dp/1617290084/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1400267855&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=specification+by+example">Specification By Example</a>) or just tying your automation to acceptance criteria in <a href="http://www.telerik.com/teampulse">Telerik TeamPulse</a>.</p> <p>Cucumber is one popular tool that lets teams create text specifications over the top of drivers like the Telerik Testing Framework or WebDriver. Cucumber lets you write things like:</p> <pre><code>Given I am logged on as a payroll administrator When I edit an employee&rsquo;s pay rate Then I am able to update their salary </code></pre> <p>Below is a similar scenario using an automated test in Telerik Test Studio tied to acceptance criteria in TeamPulse.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]abf6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]abf6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="tp_ts_criteria_970" alt="tp_ts_criteria_970" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]abf6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <h3>Acceptance criteria TeamPulse and Test Studio</h3> <p>Using automation in this fashion helps you ensure that what you&rsquo;re building is as close as possible to what the customer is actually looking for. You&rsquo;re not only gaining a regression safety net, you&rsquo;re also gaining confidence that the system is functioning as your customer expects. You&rsquo;ll also gain a bit of living documentation if you&rsquo;re taking care in how you write your acceptance criteria.</p> <h2>UI Tests? Why Me? Let The Testers Do That!</h2> <p>Too often UI tests are seen in the domain of the &ldquo;test team.&rdquo; Long years of hard knocks and struggling (or outright failing) automation efforts have finally started to bring about some much-needed change in how and where UI tests are being created. Successful teams are bringing testing and development activities and roles much closer together&ndash;and many teams are outright mixing them totally in &ldquo;whole team testing&rdquo; delivery flows.</p> <p>Even if developers don&rsquo;t create the UI tests themselves, they&rsquo;re in a great spot to help the automated suites be extremely successful.</p> <p>Developers can make the UI much more testable by scattering good IDs across critical elements. They can also help UI tests deal with tricky asynchronous situations by providing appropriate latches when async calls have completed. Need APIs to help leverage system functionality for test setup and teardown? Devs are all over that.</p> <p>As an example, the following snippet of code will update the ID of a row in a Kendo UI Grid to be based on the ContactName text from a record:</p> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">dataBound: </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">function</code> <code style="color: #000000;">(dataBoundEvent) {</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">gridWidget = dataBoundEvent.sender;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">dataSource = gridWidget.dataSource;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">$.each(gridWidget.items(), </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">function</code> <code style="color: #000000;">(index, item) {</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">uid = $(item).data(</code><code style="color: blue;">"uid"</code><code style="color: #000000;">);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">dataItem = dataSource.getByUid(uid);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">$(item).attr(</code><code style="color: blue;">"id"</code><code style="color: #000000;">, dataItem.ContactName);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">});</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></div> </div> <br /> <h2>Bring on the Geek: Performance and Load Testing</h2> <p>For devs who feel general UI automation isn&rsquo;t cool enough, think about a couple seriously geeky types of testing: Performance and Load. UI testing is the gateway to seeing just how well your system holds up in a real environment. Get a few UI tests in place, and it&rsquo;s an easy step to begin working with CPU utilization rates, cache hits, disk bottlenecks, and time to first byte metrics.</p> <h2>Bringing It Home: Get Testing!</h2> <p>Developers can bring great value to their organizations by learning the fundamentals of UI automation. Even if developers don&rsquo;t do the majority of the UI automation, understanding how the process and tools work means devs can ensure the system is as testable as possible. Devs can lend a hand in writing the automation to ensure it&rsquo;s as robust as possible, and they can utilize their system knowledge to help flesh out critical aspects of the test infrastructure.</p> <p>Tools like <a href="http://www.telerik.com/teststudio/visual-studio-testing-plugin-benefits">Telerik Test Studio&rsquo;s plugin for Visual Studio</a> make it simple for developers to quickly help adding value to their automated test suites. Developers can either quickly add value to automation written by testers, or they can quickly build stable, flexible automated tests themselves. Moreover, developers will see great additional features if they&rsquo;re also using Telerik UI controls. Wrappers for many Telerik controls provide additional functionality to instantly verify things like row counts in Rad or Kendo UI grids, check the count of nodes in a KendoTreeView, or deal with complex scope changes of elements on the page.<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d5f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d5f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="grid_xlator_narrow" alt="grid_xlator_narrow" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d5f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span></p> <p><span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e1f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e1f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="tree_xlator" alt="tree_xlator" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e1f6ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br />Have we gotten your interest yet? In future posts, I plan to dive into the specifics for some of Telerik&rsquo;s popular controls. You&rsquo;ll be seeing more details around Kendo UI and AJAX controls.</p> <p>Are you finding UI testing useful in your organizations? How are you working together with your testers? Let us know in the comments!</p> <p><em>This article was originally published at <a href="http://blogs.telerik.com/blogs/14-06-04/why-should-developers-help-with-user-interface-tests-">http://blogs.telerik.com/blogs/14-06-04/why-should-developers-help-with-user-interface-tests-</a></em></p> <br /> <br />
Why Web Components Arent Ready for Production Yet|Web|<p>Web components are the new hotness. And now that a complete web components implementation landed in Chrome 36, we finally have stable, unprefixed, unflagged version to try out. But, although web components are certainly something to be excited about, and a technology worth experimenting with, that doesn&rsquo;t mean that they&rsquo;re ready to use in your production applications &mdash; because for most applications, they&rsquo;re not.</p> <p>In this article you&rsquo;ll see why that is. We&rsquo;ll discuss the current issues with using web components in production, and what needs to be done to solve them.</p> <h2>Browser support</h2> <p>The obvious reason to avoid web components is browser support. Although web components landed in Chrome 36, they only have partial support in Firefox, and they are not present in Safari or IE. Because cross-browser support won&rsquo;t be possible for a very long time, <a href="https://lists.webkit.org/pipermail/webkit-dev/2013-May/024894.html">if it happens at all</a>, a polyfill is a long-term necessity for developers that want to use web components outside of Chrome.</p> <p>And there is seemingly good news on this front: the Polymer team maintains a <a href="http://www.polymer-project.org/docs/start/platform.html">complete set of polyfills</a> collectively known as &ldquo;The Platform&rdquo;. But although <a href="http://www.polymer-project.org/docs/start/platform.html">Polymer&rsquo;s official documentation</a> makes it seem like getting cross-browser web components support is as easy as including platform.js, things aren&rsquo;t quite that simple.</p> <h2>All polyfills aren&rsquo;t created equal</h2> <p>Although developers tend to think of a polyfill as a polyfill, the complexity of the implementations can vary widely depending on the technology being polyfilled. APIs that are syntactic sugar for existing APIs tend to be the easiest to implement. For instance you can write a <a href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Function/bind#Compatibility"><code>Function.prototype.bind()</code> polyfill</a> in a handful of lines of code, and a <a href="https://github.com/remy/polyfills/blob/6e87470526c496c0fc53fa87ed5a825eff61f1f3/classList.js"><code>classList</code> polyfill</a> polyfill in about 70 lines of code.</p> <p>APIs that add entirely new behavior, or that include CSS syntax changes, tend to be harder to write. For example consider a pointer events polyfill. Because pointer events work with the <code>touch-action</code> CSS property, and because browsers ignore CSS rules for properties they do not understand, pointer event polyfills have to get creative. <a href="https://github.com/polymer/PointerEvents">Polymer&rsquo;s pointer events polyfill</a> requires you to use a <code>touch-action</code> <em>attribute</em> on elements &mdash; e.g. <code>&lt;div touch-action="none"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</code> &mdash; and forego the CSS declaration entirely. <a href="http://handjs.codeplex.com/">Microsoft&rsquo;s HandJS polyfill</a> does a text-based search of all <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;link rel="stylesheet"&gt;</code> tags for the <code>touch-action</code> property, and then replicates that functionality in JavaScript &mdash; which obviously has non-trivial performance implications.</p> <p>The point here being, some technologies are harder to polyfill than others. Why is this relevant? Well, as it turns out, polyfilling web components is the king of all polyfilling challenges. Simply put, writing a web components polyfill is absurdly difficult, and the inherit complexity has non-trivial implications on the viability of using web components polyfills in production. Let&rsquo;s look at some examples to demonstrate this.</p> <h2>The insanity that is polyfilling web components</h2> <p>To start, consider the encapsulation that the shadow DOM specification allows. When HTML elements live within a shadow root they are hidden from functions such as <code>querySelector()</code> and <code>querySelectorAll()</code>. For example, in the following screenshot <code>querySelectorAll()</code> does not find the <code>&lt;h1&gt;</code> element because it&rsquo;s within a shadow root:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]19f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]19f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f5942615758545a2e706e67" alt="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f5942615758545a2e706e67" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]19f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p>Think for a moment how you would polyfill this behavior. Crazy, right? But, if you add Polymer&rsquo;s platform.js to the same example, it unbelievably works. That is, <code>querySelectorAll()</code> does not find elements within a shadow root &mdash; in browsers that have no concept of a shadow root. The following screenshot shows this behavior in Safari:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]25f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]25f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f657879325341542e706e67" alt="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f657879325341542e706e67" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]25f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p>I had to run this a few times to verify my own sanity, as I had no idea how this was even possible. How does a polyfill do this?</p> <p>The answer is, Polymer&rsquo;s shadow DOM polyfill wraps a large number of DOM methods &mdash; <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/ShadowDOM/blob/master/src/wrappers/Document.js">at least 25 of them</a> &mdash; with a series of customized shims that exclude elements that reside within its internal list of shadow roots, and it does this for <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/ShadowDOM/tree/master/src/wrappers">over 30 HTML element interfaces</a>. No, I&rsquo;m not kidding.</p> <p>And we&rsquo;re just getting started with the crazy polyfilling challenges that web components present. The shadow DOM specification also defines a number of new CSS concepts for exposing and selecting elements that reside within shadow roots. So, because browsers discard CSS selectors and rules they don&rsquo;t understand, this means that polyfills must resort to text-based searches of <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;link rel="stylesheet"&gt;</code> tags. This 38-line block of regular expressions <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/platform-dev/blob/fe5427b747e5bca3dde9ba891cb33d279faff3f9/src/ShadowCSS.js#L544-581">defined in platform.js&rsquo; source</a> should give you an idea of the difficulty of doing this:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]34f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]34f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f473659467072742e706e67" alt="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f473659467072742e706e67" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]34f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <h2>Why this stuff matters?</h2> <p>I want to make it clear that the code examples I show above are not meant to be a reflection on the engineering effort put forth by Google, but rather an attempt to show the complexity inherent to reimplementing web components in JavaScript. The web components specification aims to give hooks into browser internals, so it makes sense that it&rsquo;s nearly impossible to replicate that functionality in JavaScript. Given this, the job the Google did with the platform polyfills is <em>extraordinary</em>.</p> <p>But, given that Google has already done the hard work, why should you care about how difficult it was? Because the complexity has several adverse effects on the feasibility of using these polyfills in production. Let&rsquo;s look at each of these issues in turn.</p> <h3>Not everything is supported</h3> <p>The first issue is, even with some of the code examples shown above, Polymer&rsquo;s polyfills do not support all the functionality that web components offer (most notably shadow DOM), or as <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/platform-dev/blob/fe5427b747e5bca3dde9ba891cb33d279faff3f9/src/ShadowCSS.js#L14-16">the source says</a>:</p> <blockquote> <p>&ldquo;The intention here is to support only the styling features which can be relatively simply implemented. The goal is to allow users to avoid the most obvious pitfalls and do so without compromising performance significantly.&rdquo;</p> </blockquote> <p>Although I disagree with Google&rsquo;s interpretation of &ldquo;relatively simply&rdquo;, I understand the sentiment here, as truly polyfilling all of shadow DOM would require rewriting CSS in JavaScript, and <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/platform-dev/blob/fe5427b747e5bca3dde9ba891cb33d279faff3f9/src/ShadowCSS.js#L90">even Google agrees</a> that goes too far.</p> <p>The problem is, from a developer&rsquo;s perspective it&rsquo;s hard to know what will work and what won&rsquo;t. The only documentation of this I can find is hidden, such as this <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/platform-dev/blob/fe5427b747e5bca3dde9ba891cb33d279faff3f9/src/ShadowCSS.js#L10-123">113-line comment in a source file discussing which portions of shadow DOM&rsquo;s CSS is supported</a>.</p> <p>Remember the example above of <code>querySelectorAll()</code> not finding an <code>&lt;h1&gt;</code>? That same example has different behavior when styling that <code>&lt;h1&gt;</code> in a <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> tag. The following shows the difference in Chrome and Safari:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]47f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]47f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f63514d57706b662e706e67" alt="687474703a2f2f692e696d6775722e636f6d2f63514d57706b662e706e67" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]47f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <h3>The polyfills are also big</h3> <p>The next issue is sheer file size. As of version 0.3.3, the platform polyfills comprise 151K of JavaScript, 44K gzipped. If you choose to use Polymer on top of the polyfills, you can add another 66K, 20K gzipped. For comparison, people complain incessantly about the size of jQuery, which is 29K gzipped.</p> <p>Polymer does do a good job of separating the polyfills into separate modules so you can pick and choose the ones you need, but in practice almost no one actually does that (the one exception being <a href="http://www.x-tags.org/">Mozilla&rsquo;s X-Tags library</a>). All of Polymer&rsquo;s elements, and most (all?) of the elements listed on <a href="http://customelements.io/">http://customelements.io/</a> and <a href="http://component.kitchen/">http://component.kitchen/</a> depend on Polymer, which depends on the platform in its entirety.</p> <h3>Performance considerations</h3> <p>Beyond the overhead of shipping the polyfills across the network, there&rsquo;s also the time it takes for the browser to parse and interpret the JavaScript code, which is a <a href="http://flippinawesome.org/2014/03/10/is-jquery-too-big-for-mobile/">known performance bottleneck on mobile devices</a>. Polymer&rsquo;s documentation states that they <a href="http://www.polymer-project.org/resources/faq.html#performancestuff">do not yet do performance benchmarking</a>, which is unfortunate considering that many of the things Polymer&rsquo;s polyfills do are inherently slow.</p> <p>One performance issue worth specifically discussing is the numerous requests generated by HTML imports. The idea behind HTML imports is great: a single .html file that contains everything you need &mdash; templates, CSS, JavaScript, other HTML, and so forth. As such, it&rsquo;s relatively common to see <a href="https://github.com/PolymerLabs/google-map/blob/d41c31802c7319884c9e8885a2c1ebc7e5663ba6/google-map.html">an HTML import that depends on several other resources</a>, each of which must be resolved over HTTP.</p> <p>In browsers that support HTML imports natively &mdash; i.e. in Chrome &mdash; the browser can resolve these resources using parallel connections, usually ~6&ndash;8 per hostname, as well as leverage <a href="http://www.html5rocks.com/en/tutorials/internals/howbrowserswork/#Speculative_parsing">speculative parsing algorithms</a> to optimize the loading of these resources. This works great in Chrome. But in all other browsers, when HTML imports aren&rsquo;t natively supported, polyfills must resort to queued up XHRs, which can be painfully slow.</p> <p>This isn&rsquo;t very noticeable on a demo of one web component on a high-end development machine, but it shows on pages with multiple components &mdash; especially on slow networks and mobile devices. The best example of this is actually Polymer&rsquo;s site itself, which uses Polymer to build everything you see. Visit <a href="http://www.polymer-project.org/">http://www.polymer-project.org/</a> in any browser that isn&rsquo;t Chrome 36+ and see the performance issues for yourselves (iOS Safari and IE Mobile are particularly bad).</p> <p>Now, there is some good news on this front, as some tooling is emerging to aide with the performance issues. <a href="https://github.com/Polymer/vulcanize">Vulcanize</a> is a build tool from the Polymer team specifically for HTML imports. In a nutshell, you pass Vulcanize an HTML file and it outputs that HTML file with all HTML imports inlined, including deep dependencies. For example, the following inlines all imports in an index.html file, and places the output in a built.html file:</p> <pre><code>$ vulcanize -o built.html index.html </code></pre> <p>That being said, build tools such as Vulcanize are still in their infancy. A lot more research needs to be done to show how to incorporate a tool such as this in to existing development processes and workflows.</p> <h2>So what does this all mean?</h2> <p>Web components and Polymer are exciting technologies that may fundamentally change the way we develop web applications, but because of the large performance gap between browsers that support the technologies natively (aka Chrome 36+) and those that don&rsquo;t (aka every other browser), it will be difficult for most developers to use web components until they&rsquo;re implemented everywhere, and there&rsquo;s no way of knowing how long that will be.</p> <p>Here are a few things I think would help the adoption of web components:</p> <ul> <li><strong>Browser support</strong>&mdash;Duh. But seriously, web components without any polyfills perform great in Chrome 36+; it&rsquo;s the polyfilling that causes the issues.</li> <li><strong>Better modularity</strong>&mdash;99% of published web components rely on Polymer, which relies on platform.js, which means always downloading 66K of gzipped JavaScript. jQuery doesn&rsquo;t get a free pass for 29K, so Polymer shouldn&rsquo;t get one for 66K (mental note: register youmightnotneedpolymer.com). Polymer could be better about micromanaging its dependencies, and documenting how to do that in third-party components. Shadow DOM is easily the biggest and most complex web components polyfill, and many components don&rsquo;t even need it &mdash; yet, 99% of them are downloading and parsing it.</li> <li><strong>Performance benchmarks</strong>&mdash;If the Polymer team expects people to use web components in real apps, then they should start benchmarking the polyfills in the browsers developers need to support, especially mobile ones.</li> </ul> <p>Bear in mind that none of this is especially surprising given that Polymer is still <a href="http://www.polymer-project.org/resources/faq.html#readiness">officially designated as a &ldquo;developer preview&rdquo;</a>. I&rsquo;m confident that better tools, better modularity, and better performance will come in time, but Polymer and web components are still really new in the web development world.</p> <h2>How This Affects Kendo UI</h2> <p>At Telerik we get a lot of questions about when Kendo UI will support web components, as UI widgets are seen as an ideal use case for the web components model. But, although we certainly look forward to the day we can provide <code>&lt;kendo-calendar&gt;</code> as an API, we have to weigh that against the need to provide high quality and performant components for our users in the browsers we support.</p> <p>We&rsquo;re continuously researching Polymer and the web components specification so that we can leverage the benefits they provide when appropriate, and the research presented above is a direct result of that. Simply put, Kendo UI does not support web components today because we cannot provide high quality and performant components using them.</p> <p>We are, however, concentrating on making our widgets as easy as possible to use with production-ready technologies; therefore, although you can&rsquo;t write <code>&lt;kendo-calendar&gt;</code> yet, you can write <code>&lt;div data-role="calendar"&gt;</code> using our <a href="http://docs.telerik.com/kendo-ui/howto/declarative_initialization">declarative data bindings</a>, as well as <a href="http://kendo-labs.github.io/angular-kendo/#/Calendar"><code>&lt;div kendo-calendar="..." k-ng-model="..."&gt;</code></a> using our <a href="http://kendo-labs.github.io/angular-kendo/#/">comprehensive set of Angular directives</a>. We&rsquo;re also constantly listening to what our users want. If you&rsquo;re interested in seeing web components and/or Polymer in Kendo UI, let us know in <a href="http://kendoui-feedback.telerik.com/forums/127393-telerik-kendo-ui-feedback">our feedback portal</a>.</p> <h2>What&rsquo;s next</h2> <p>This article purposely focused on the feasibility of using web components in production today, and did not touch on whether web components are actually <em>worth</em> using &mdash; that discussion is coming in a future article.</p>
Wicked Smart Dummy Data for .NET|.NET|<p>We all do it. We all feel guilty about it later. We all pretend to not see it when we see presenters do it live or when it&rsquo;s done in a video.</p> <p>It&rsquo;s demo data. Useless, often completely random, sometimes requiring a disclaimer that it isn&rsquo;t based on real life so that nobody gets offended or calls the lawyers. But we&rsquo;re surrounded by demo data, or testing data, or &ldquo;I just wanted to see what the component looks like&rdquo; data and if you are reading this article you are probably guilty too. Don&rsquo;t worry, nobody minds the ten minutes it takes sometimes to write up a quick class and generator for some data. There isn&rsquo;t some great anti-achievement clock running to record every time us developers spend some time lining up a few ducks so we can properly see what a view looks like with data. And if there was, we&rsquo;d all be in trouble.</p> <h2>Take Some Time</h2> <p>Components like the RadGridView (any .Net flavor) don&rsquo;t just offer standard grid-y things like sorting, filtering, and grouping, but also a fast and responsive data engine with cool bells and whistles like multi-cell selection, a variety of exports, and interoperability with other components in the suite and platform.</p> <p>However, now that you&rsquo;ve got your Grid component working, you need some data to display. Since the senior devs get upset when you start grabbing data from random sources around the office (guilty as charged :D), you decide to write up a few quick classes to display &ldquo;remote data&rdquo;.</p> <p>~10 minutes later you&rsquo;ve got a class and a quick generator to populate out a list, it&rsquo;s tied to RadGridView, and you&rsquo;ve got a functional control to bring to the powers that be to show off the wisdom they made in purchasing a Telerik UI suite. The demo goes so well that there are questions -</p> <p><em>&ldquo;Can it display hierarchical data?&rdquo;</em> &ndash; Well yes, but the class isn&rsquo;t set up to be hierarchical. Give me a few minutes to get that setup&hellip;</p> <p><em>&ldquo;Are there charts? We need financial charts!&rdquo;</em> &ndash; Sure, I haven&rsquo;t looked at them, once I make that class hierarchical I&rsquo;ll work up some financial data as well&hellip;</p> <p><em>&ldquo;Excellent, you&rsquo;ll be ready just in time for your review!&rdquo;</em> &ndash; Review?! But I&rsquo;ve got a ton of demo data to write up!</p> <p>You can see where this is headed.</p> <h2>Save Some Time</h2> <p>Nobody wants to rush through getting demo data ready for a new component suite when the powers that be are sitting around waiting for your demo so they can finish your review. That&rsquo;s pressure!</p> <p>Understanding this is a daily risk developers have to deal with, I&rsquo;d like to introduce a small side-project to help users with Telerik .Net components either get off the ground or whip up a quick view to test a control. The <a href="https://github.com/EvanHutnick/telerik.ui.net.demolibrary">Telerik.UI.Net.DemoLibrary project is live and public on Github</a> as I write this and was also used (but not highlighted) in our previous post about <a href="http://developer.telerik.com/featured/net-the-bad-parts/">.NET &ndash; The Bad Parts</a>.</p> <p>Let&rsquo;s take a look inside to see what resources are available within the library. Assuming we&rsquo;re all familiar with Visual Studio, here&rsquo;s a class diagram of the classes contained in the .01 Alpha version:</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]7ff7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]7ff7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="dummy-daat1" alt="dummy-daat1" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]7ff7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br />Each of these class types has a generator for creating either individual items of a given class or a List&lt;T&gt; of the class. If we go straight to Solution Explorer we can see what some of the Generators look like:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]8bf7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]8bf7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="dummy-data2" alt="dummy-data2" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]8bf7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p>Neat, right? As you can see the alpha tag applies since some generators have optional parameters and some require a concrete parameter. Over time I plan to iron out these rough edges, but I wanted to first get a sense about which would be more useful for developers. Since it&rsquo;s on Github, however, feel free to recompile with any tweaks or changes locally, or provide me with feedback and I can look to include it in library updates.</p> <p>Since we had some code-reuse issues within code generation, there are also some auxiliary utilities which made sense being public. You can find these within the Static folder:</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]97f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]97f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="dummy-data3" alt="dummy-data3" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]97f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p>So in case you need a quick list of names, countries, cities, books, or want either a random length string (TextGenerator.GetRandomLengthText) or a random sentence (.GetRandomText()) you&rsquo;re covered. Need an email? Use .GetEmail() to produce a name from the names list &lsquo;@fakegmail.com&rsquo; email address so it will pass email validation. TextGenerator may get split from a TextSource and move to the Generators folder, but again, alpha version and feedback is welcome.</p> <h2>Back to Work!</h2> <p>At the end of the day this won&rsquo;t generate the same type of buzz as a new product release, but if you&rsquo;re a developer like the rest of us, little utilities like these which save time and make the day a little easier are worth their weight in bytes. So head over to <a href="https://github.com/EvanHutnick/telerik.ui.net.demolibrary">Github</a>, grab a copy of the library, submit feedback on what you&rsquo;d find useful, and have fun saving time while developing.</p>
Web Menus for the Modern Touch World|Web|<p>You&rsquo;re on the road with your smartphone/tablet and pull up a website in your browser (not while you are driving, of course!). The fancy new restaurant in town lures you in with mouth-watering menus or that game score you so desperately want to follow on a sports site. While the beautifully designed website loads up fine, you realize that it is largely unusable with touch! Dropdown menus don&rsquo;t pop out or clever interactions, that may&nbsp;work fine with a mouse pointer, fall flat on their face. You are not alone &amp; plenty of us experience the same frustrations everyday. Let&rsquo;s see how this looks like from a developer&rsquo;s perspective.</p> <h2>The Problem</h2> <p>Coming from the desktop era where keyboard and mouse/trackpad were primary input devices, touch represents a whole new opportunity and a slew of challenges. The touch paradigm adds new interactivity options while complicating some traditional ones. To keep users happy, we web developers need to embrace touch, while maintaining existing interactions. Touch is everywhere these days &ndash; smartphones, tablets, laptops, hybrids, ultrabooks, TVs, kiosks, etc. &ndash; so we need to account for it.</p> <p>Now, I realize we are opening a can of worms here &ndash; touch for web platforms has been thoroughly debated, researched and written about &ndash; make sure to check out some of the links in the &ldquo;Resources&rdquo; section for deeper understanding of touch interactions for web. However, in this article I&nbsp;cover some of the&nbsp;broader issues to keep&nbsp;in mind, some known workarounds and how&nbsp;the right UI controls can help.</p> <h2>Some Caveats &amp; Solutions</h2> <p>The good news for web developers is that touch for web applications is nothing new and there is a lot&nbsp;support out of the box. iOS was one of the first popular mobile platforms to have dedicated touch APIs built for web browsers but nowadays touch support is ubiquitous in mobile browsers. Touch interactions are supported seamlessly in Chrome, Firefox, Safari, IE (which supports Pointer events) and other browsers. However, touch support does not take away the challenges of coding for touch interactions in web applications. Let&rsquo;s look into some common pitfalls and solutions:</p> <ol> <li>Supporting touch natively does not mean dropping support for mouse/trackpad interactions. With devices such as Windows 8 Surface/hybrids/ultrabooks or Chromebooks, users may have both touch and mouse, and may even be using them simultaneously. Our web apps need to handle both and be adaptive. Also, touch support introduces new interaction paradigms &ndash; like multitouch or&nbsp;touch and hold; let&rsquo;s use these to our advantage as it fits the user experience.</li> <li>Touch implementation on mobile web browsers often imitates mouse clicks, but there are some differences. A single tap usually raises the following events in succession:<br /> <strong><strong>TouchStart | TouchMove | TouchEnd | MouseOver | MouseMove | MouseDown | MouseUp | Click.<br /> </strong></strong>So, as you can see, if we do not take explicit action, mobile browsers will eventually trigger a mouse click in response to a finger tap &ndash; this is good. But where we easily get into trouble is when the touch interaction&nbsp;also triggers actions on one of the events raised prior to the mouse click. A common technique to get around this is to fire off the <code>preventDefault()</code> JavaScript code inside the touch event handlers, so as to suppress the default mouse emulation.</li> <li>Touch interaction with our big fat fingers is very different from the precision control offered by the mouse/trackpad. So, let&rsquo;s keep this basic difference in mind as we design our web apps for touch. Touchability usually entails spacing between elements &amp; enough padding so that the user can touch comfortably. There are plenty of usability studies &amp; mobile friendly CSS (Cascading StyleSheets) themes to help us out in this department.</li> <li>I saved the best for the last &ndash; hover with touch. The mouse pointer metaphor separated the cursor position from an active selection, allowing developers to use hover states to hide/show information that might be pertinent to the user. However, most touch interfaces right now do not detect a finger &lsquo;hovering&rsquo; over a target &ndash; this is a problem!</li> </ol> <h2>That pesky Hover</h2> <p>So, let&rsquo;s talk about how we support&nbsp;hover in a&nbsp;touch world. A common pattern on the Web is to hide interactive content behind mouse hover. For example, a user can point to an element with a mouse and a menu is shown below it. Hover is typically enabled by using either the CSS <code>:hover</code> pseudo-class or the <code>onMouseOver</code> DOM (Document Object Model) event. Since, there is no exact physical metaphor to hover with a finger, we need to be a little careful in our usage. If our web UI has prolific usage of hover effect and that is the only interaction available&nbsp;to hide/show specific content, we will have a problem on&nbsp;touch devices.</p> <p>Interestingly, all is not lost if we use hover; many touch implementations in mobile browsers may still trigger the hover effect. Usually, tapping a DOM element makes it toggle to CSS <code>:active</code> while the finger is down, and it also acquires the CSS <em>:hover</em> state. With IE, the CSS <em>:hover</em> is only in effect while the user&rsquo;s finger is down; WebKit browsers keep the <em>:hover</em> in effect until the next tap or mouse move. So this is partially good news, except for the case where you want to keep hover separate from activation. A tap is essentially a hover and activation in one action; activation (like navigating to a link) trumps hover thus impacting the user experience.</p> <p>One way around this hover effect is to switch to something more generic, like an&nbsp;<code>onClick</code> event to toggle content visibility. Another, &nbsp;possibly more robust alternative is to fall back to a new behavior from the <em>W3C (World-Wide Web Consortium) ARIA (Accessible Rich Internet Applications Suite)</em> suite. W3C Aria defines a way to make Web content and Web applications more accessible to people with disabilities. It especially helps with dynamic content and advanced user interface controls developed with Ajax, HTML, JavaScript, and related technologies. Modern web browsers will honor <code>aria-haspopup</code> property to simulate hover on DOM elements with hidden interactive content. Let&rsquo;s write some code, which is based upon&nbsp;an IE10 sample from&nbsp;MSDN:</p> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">a</code> <code style="color: #808080;">href</code><code style="color: #000000;">=</code><code style="color: blue;">"/news/sports"</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;Sports&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">a</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 21px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">ul</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 9px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">ul</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></div> </div> <br /> <p>The above markup renders a little menu/list with desired hover effects to show/hide content. Mouse-keyboard users do not notice any difference and hover works as expected. For touch users, the first tap on the DOM element triggers the hover state, until another tap somewhere else or a navigation occurs. The <em>aria-haspopup</em> property conveniently masks the subsequent mouse emulation events to simulate hover on touch-enabled web browsers. Nifty, isn&rsquo;t it?</p> <h2>Telerik Controls to the Rescue</h2> <p>Creating adaptive web and mobile applications is difficult enough, before having to worry about&nbsp;low-level touch support for web menus. Thankfully, no matter which Telerik web control suite you pick up to use, menus&nbsp;work seamlessly between desktop world with standard mouse inputs and touch devices. Let&rsquo;s dig in to see how.</p> <h2>Kendo UI</h2> <p><a href="http://www.telerik.com/kendo-ui">Kendo UI</a> is an HTML and JavaScript&nbsp;toolset that includes over&nbsp;70 UI widgets, in addition to robust framework support.&nbsp;&nbsp;In addition, <a href="http://www.telerik.com/download/kendo-ui-core">Kendo UI Core</a>, which includes over 40 popular UI widgets,&nbsp;is now free and open source!&nbsp;Kendo UI Professional includes&nbsp;a number of advanced UI widgets as well as server-side wrappers and quality support.</p> <p>Now, let&rsquo;s take a look at the&nbsp;Kendo UI Web <strong>Menu</strong> widget which is a simple dropdown menu control for navigation or organization, but&nbsp;on steroids. &nbsp;The&nbsp;Kendo UI Menu demos are&nbsp;<a href="http://demos.telerik.com/kendo-ui/web/menu/index.html">available here</a> and <a href="http://docs.telerik.com/kendo-ui/api/web/menu">detailed documentation here</a>.</p> <p>Head to the <a href="http://demos.telerik.com/kendo-ui/web/menu/index.html">Kendo UI Menu demo site</a> on a machine with mouse/trackpad and then on a tablet/smartphone with just touch. Do you see that it works seamlessly across both? For mouse/trackpad, hover shows/hides content and for touch, the first tap triggers the hover state.</p> <p>One of the nice things about the Kendo UI Demos is the support for built-in Dojos! Click on the &ldquo;Edit this example&rdquo; button and you will be taken to <a href="http://trykendoui.telerik.com/">Telerik Kendo UI Dojos</a> with the Kendo UI references pulled in via Telerik CDNs (Content Delivery Networks) and the code ready for you to slice and dice (screenshot below). &nbsp;Also, results of your code changes are readily visible in the Results pane, along with switchable skins to simulate web and mobile (iOS/Android/Windows Phone) platforms, as shown in the screenshot.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]bff7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]bff7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="tp_ts_criteria_970" alt="tp_ts_criteria_970" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]bff7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br />Results:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]cbf7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]cbf7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="KendoUIDojoResults-1024x851" alt="KendoUIDojoResults-1024x851" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]cbf7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br />As you make subsequent code changes, the URL gets a unique GUID &ndash; so there&rsquo;s no need to host your code in order to try out on various devices; share the URL and run with it! If you want parity between the desktop and mobile functionality of your Kendo UI Menu, try this piece of code:<br /> <br /> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;Sub Item 3&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 24px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">ul</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699;
