font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;Sub Item 1&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 36px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;Sub Item 2&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 36px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;Sub Item 3&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 24px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">ul</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">li</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">ul</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">script</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">$("#menu").kendoMenu({</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 33px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">openOnClick: true,</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 33px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">closeOnClick: true</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">});</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">script</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></div> </div> <br /> <p>The <code>openOnClick</code> property of the Kendo UI Menu stops the pop-out on hover and triggers it only on click and the <code>closeOnClick</code> property specifies whether sub-menus should close after item selection. Now, the mouse interactions will behave just like touch. I&rsquo;m not saying that&rsquo;s the way to go, but just an option for you.</p> <p>Of course, my assumption with the Kendo UI Menu is that you have a web app that you simply want to work everywhere without any more effort to restyle the menu for a mobile look and feel. If time and budget permits and you are looking for an even better mobile user experience, try the Kendo UI <strong>Mobile Drawer</strong> widget. The Mobile Drawer is a slide out menu that you are probably familiar with (screenshot below). You can check out the Kendo UI Drawer usage in the <a href="http://demos.telerik.com/kendo-ui/mobile/drawer/index.html">documentation</a>.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e9f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e9f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="KendoUIDrawer-564x1024" alt="KendoUIDrawer-564x1024" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e9f7ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <h2>UI for ASP.NET Ajax</h2> <p>If you&rsquo;re building web apps in the ASP.NET world, take a look at Telerik&rsquo;s UI for ASP.NET Ajax suite. It contains over 80 performance-tuned ASP.NET controls with mobile support and adaptive rendering. You can <a href="http://www.telerik.com/products/aspnet-ajax.aspx">learn more about them</a> and feel free to check out the free trial.</p> <p>Now, the UI for ASP.NET <strong>Menu</strong> control is feature-rich and helps render a lightweight HTML5 menu structure for your navigation needs. You can <a href="http://www.telerik.com/products/aspnet-ajax/menu.aspx">learn more about the ASP.NET Menu</a> or <a href="http://demos.telerik.com/aspnet-ajax/menu/examples/overview/defaultcs.aspx">check out the demos</a>.</p> <p>With the Telerik UI for ASP.NET Menu, you can just leave it as is and it includes hover support on mouse/trackpad and click support on touch. Better still, if you want to make the mobile experience even more touch-friendly, use the built-in adaptive rendering behavior. Let&rsquo;s write a little code:</p> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">telerik:RadMenu</code> <code style="color: #808080;">ID</code><code style="color: #000000;">=</code><code style="color: blue;">"RadMenu1"</code> <code style="color: #808080;">runat</code><code style="color: #000000;">=</code><code style="color: blue;">"server"</code> <code style="color: #808080;">RenderMode</code><code style="color: #000000;">=</code><code style="color: blue;">"Mobile"</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">Items</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">...</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">...</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">Items</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px !important;"><code style="color: #000000;">&lt;/</code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">telerik:RadMenu</code><code style="color: #000000;">&gt;</code></span></div> </div> <br />You can enable the mobile layout of the control by setting the&nbsp;<code>RenderMode</code>&nbsp;property of the Menu to&nbsp;<em>Mobile</em>. The menu immediately renders like the Kendo UI Mobile Drawer.&nbsp;To cover the rendering of both mobile and desktop devices, you could set the&nbsp;<em>RenderMode</em>&nbsp;property to&nbsp;<em>Auto</em>. This option does browser viewport detection and will automatically decide how to render the menu on a smartphone, tablet or PC, as shown in screenshot.&nbsp;For more accurate device detection, you could reference the&nbsp;<a href="http://www.telerik.com/products/aspnet-ajax/device-detection-framework.aspx">Device Detection Framework</a>, which ensures that the control is rendered every time with appropriate UI according to the device screen size.<br /> <h2>Conclusion</h2> <p>The web should simply work whether you are browsing with a mouse/trackpad or on touch devices, with a little forethought from the developer. No longer should we be frustrated with foiled interactions and hover failure on web menus with touch devices. Little tweaks go a long way towards the perfect mobile user experience. If you choose to use Telerik UI controls, be it Kendo UI for the HTML5/JavaScript world or UI for ASP.NET Ajax, your menus always work, adapting to the user&rsquo;s device. Let&rsquo;s make a beautiful, touchable web!</p> <h2>Resources for further reading</h2> <ol> <li><a href="http://www.html5rocks.com/en/mobile/touch/">Multi-touch Web Development</a></li> <li><a href="http://www.html5rocks.com/en/mobile/touchandmouse/">Touch And Mouse</a></li> <li><a href="http://www.w3.org/TR/touch-events/">W3C Touch Events</a></li> <li><a href="http://www.w3.org/WAI/intro/aria">W3C WAI-ARIA Overview</a></li> <li><a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/ie/jj152135%28v=vs.85%29.aspx">Using aria-haspopup to simulate hover on touch-enabled devices</a></li> <li><a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/jj152141%28v=vs.85%29.aspx">How to simulate hover on touch-enabled devices</a></li> <li><a href="http://www.telerik.com/forums/menu-onclick-open-close">Menu onClick Open &amp; Close</a></li> </ol> <br />
Surviving Australia with Telerik AppManager|Testing|<p>See that spider in the photo above? I caught this &ldquo;little guy&rdquo; in my home office. I found him underneath my desk, checking out the various power cords I have. This was just one of many spiders I&rsquo;ve caught around the house. Truth be told, I&rsquo;ve been fascinated by the Australian wildlife I&rsquo;ve encountered since moving here from Canada. For example, did you know that the spiders here are so big that they have hair? Back in Canada, you&rsquo;d be lucky to see a spider larger than a penny. But here in Australia, you can see their fur. It&rsquo;s awesome! (Cue <a href="http://youtu.be/wy_TB6onHVE">&ldquo;Scared Weird Little Guys&rdquo;</a> in the background while you&rsquo;re reading this.)</p> <p>Like any resourceful Canadian developer living abroad, I&rsquo;ve built a few apps to survive whatever this country decides to throw at me (Note: Australia is a very safe place to live &ndash; those of us living here find it funny to see how people view Australia from overseas. &ldquo;ZOMG! It&rsquo;s full of spiders, snakes, and sharks!&rdquo;). In fairness, the purpose of these apps is really two-fold: to assist me in a &ldquo;deadly encounter&rdquo; and to also learn more about the <a href="http://www.telerik.com/platform">Telerik Platform</a>.</p> <p>In this article, I&rsquo;ll show you how to use <a href="http://www.telerik.com/appmanager">Telerik AppManager</a>, a new capability of the <a href="http://www.telerik.com/platform">Telerik Platform</a> that enables you to securely deploy and manage your private, mobile apps. Public availability of <strong>Telerik AppManager</strong> was <a href="http://blogs.telerik.com/appmanager/posts/14-04-24/telerik-appmanager-your-new-app-management-and-distribution-tool">announced earlier this month</a>. You can check it out &ndash; along with a whole host of other services &ndash; by signing up for a free Telerik Platform account.</p> <h2>Setting Up Workspaces</h2> <p>With my apps built and ready for user testing, I&rsquo;m now at the stage where I&rsquo;m ready to put these apps into the hands of my colleagues here in Australia. To do that, I&rsquo;m going to leverage <strong>AppManager</strong>, which enables you to securely deploy and manage your private, mobile apps. Let&rsquo;s see how to do this.</p> <p>Upon signing into the <strong>Telerik Platform</strong>, I&rsquo;m shown a list of workspaces I have created:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]5bf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]5bf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-workspaces" alt="telerik-appmanager-workspaces" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]5bf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <p>Workspaces form a collection of project types that address the various aspects of your app&rsquo;s development lifecycle including the its designs, builds, tests, and deployments. As you can see in the screenshot above, I have a few workspaces dedicated to living in Australia. I&rsquo;ve also invited colleagues to join these workspaces and assist me in building them.</p> <h2>Managing Access to Private Deployments</h2> <p>I&rsquo;m going to use <strong>AppManager</strong> to get my apps into the hands of my &ldquo;beta users&rdquo;. As stated previously, <strong>AppManager</strong> expands the capabilities of the <strong>Telerik Platform</strong> by enhancing your choice of deployment options to include private deployments. I can jump immediately into <strong>AppManager</strong> to manage private deployments of hybrid or native apps, meaning apps built outside the <strong>Telerik Platform</strong>. This includes apps built in environments like XCode, Eclipse, etc.</p> <p>When you open <strong>AppManager</strong>, you&rsquo;ll be presented with a list of apps that have been prepared for private deployments to users:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]89f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]89f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-apps" alt="telerik-appmanager-apps" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]89f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p>I&rsquo;ve built three apps &ndash; two of which are published. I&rsquo;ve also assigned these apps to groups that I&rsquo;ve created, to which I&rsquo;ve assigned users.<h3>Adding Apps</h3> <p>Adding a new app to <strong>AppManager</strong> is a simple process. First, you need to generate an app package. For iOS, you&rsquo;ll need the IPA file; for Android, you&rsquo;ll need the APK file.</p> <p>Second, you&rsquo;ll need to upload your app package to <strong>AppManager</strong> via the &ldquo;Add New App&rdquo; dialog:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]95f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]95f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-add-new-app" alt="telerik-appmanager-add-new-app" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]95f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <p>A number of attributes will be prepopulated such as the app name, version, and ID. For the remaining attributes, you&rsquo;ll need to provide values for the target platform and description. You&rsquo;ll also have to assign the app to a set of distribution groups.</p> <h3>Granting Access</h3> <p>By default, no one has access to the new app deployment. However, you can create and modify these groups easily. In the case of the apps I&rsquo;ve built, I&rsquo;ve decided that I need to provide these apps to users living in different locations of Australia. I also need to ensure that certain apps are tested out by expats (i.e. me):<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]a1f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]a1f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-groups" alt="telerik-appmanager-groups" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]a1f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p>Once I&rsquo;ve established my list of groups, I can invite and assign users to them. Here, I&rsquo;ve invited members of the team at Telerik Australia:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]adf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]adf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-users" alt="telerik-appmanager-users" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]adf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br />Users can be easily invited to deployments. All that&rsquo;s required is an email address. (More on this later.) Once registered, users can be modified to update their group memberships and to see a list of devices they&rsquo;ve registered through <strong>AppManager</strong>. For example, here&rsquo;s my profile:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]b9f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]b9f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-edit-user" alt="telerik-appmanager-edit-user" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]b9f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p>As you can see, I&rsquo;m a member of various groups and I&rsquo;ve registered my iPhone and iPad with <strong>AppBuilder</strong> for private deployments.</p> <p>It&rsquo;s worth mentioning that I can create new deployments through <a href="http://www.telerik.com/appbuilder">Telerik AppBuilder</a> directly with its built-in publication facilities:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]c5f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]c5f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="appbuilder-appmanager-deployment" alt="appbuilder-appmanager-deployment" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]c5f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <h2>Pushing Updates</h2> <p><strong>AppBuilder</strong> eliminates the need to install and setup build environments; we provide all that infrastructure for you. This includes managing your deployments after they&rsquo;ve been published to users. <strong>AppBuilder</strong> considerably simplifies updating your internal apps and pushing the latest updates to end-users:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d1f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d1f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-arachnogram-app" alt="telerik-appmanager-arachnogram-app" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d1f8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <p>Here, I&rsquo;ve published my latest app, Arachnogram. It&rsquo;s a social networking app for sharing photos of spiders in Australia. As you can see, I can upload screenshots, provide updates, and see ratings from my internal users. I can even choose to make the app unavailable by &ldquo;un-publishing&rdquo; it from <strong>AppManager</strong>.</p> <p>When my users are ready to use one of my apps from their device, they&rsquo;ll see a screen from the <strong>AppManager</strong> app that looks like this:<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]ddf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]ddf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="telerik-appmanager-client-app-screen" alt="telerik-appmanager-client-app-screen" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]ddf8ff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span> </p> <p>Here, a user can download the app directly to their device. (Note: This is done without jailbreaking your device.) They can provide ratings and receive updates when new builds are deployed. The feedback I receive from them will help me plan out my next iteration, resulting in a better set of survival apps for Australia!</p> <h2>Conclusion</h2> <p>As you can see, <strong>AppManager</strong> provides a robust solution for managing the deployment of private apps to your internal users. It&rsquo;s a great, new capability of the <strong>Telerik Platform</strong> and one that I trust will assist you in your future development. I encourage you to use it and <a href="http://feedback.telerik.com/Project/129">provide your feedback</a> to the team. We really appreciate it. As an alternative, you can connect with others in the community who are using <strong>AppManager</strong> in <a href="http://www.telerik.com/forums/appmanager">our forum</a>.</p>
Microsofts Special K  An Introduction to ASP.NET vNext|.NET|<p>At TechEd, Microsoft took the wraps off of the new ASP.NET server-side components in a package they&rsquo;re calling ASP.NET vNext. Formerly, this was called Project K, and you&rsquo;ll see some insiders use both of these terms for the next few months. This project contains some very exciting capabilties for web developers, and the promise within the coming changes is impressive.</p> <h2>Introducing OWIN</h2> <p>OWIN, <a href="http://www.owin.org">the Open Web Interface for .NET</a> is taking over the ASP.NET runtime. Microsoft is embracing the open standard of constructing and assembling web components, and allowing you to assemble as opinionated or flexible a web stack as you need. Web projects now have access to an immense collection of building blocks, and you can assemble them any way you would like.</p> <p>ASP.NET MVC vNext is now OWIN compliant. That means you can run MVC applications on a very thin Katana server, or on a Project Helios managed IIS instance. These are amazing choices that you can make, as you no longer will be obligated to load packages and assemblies that you don&rsquo;t need for your application.</p> <p>Web Applications will now be delivered with 4 distinct layers:</p> <ol> <li><em>The host application</em> &ndash; in the past this was ALWAYS Microsoft Internet Information Server (IIS). This could be IIS, IIS with Helios, Katana, or some other OWIN capable host.</li> <li><em>The CLR framework</em> &ndash; previously you would choose which version of the Microsoft.NET framework you wanted to use. This may be .NET 4.5.2, K runtime, or Mono.</li> <li><em>The Application Host</em> &ndash; previously, this would be the W3C executable. Now, this can be a host for the Visual Studio designer, or the new Microsoft.Host.Runtime.</li> <li><em>Your Application</em> &ndash; Your application now loads the former ASP.NET frameworks (MVC, WebAPI, or SignalR) as part of its dependencies.</li> </ol> <h2>MVC &ndash; Meet WebAPI, WebAPI meet MVC</h2> <p>If you&rsquo;ve been working with ASP.NET for a few years, you&rsquo;ve no doubt noticed that the MVC and WebAPI frameworks are very similar. In fact, they use the same Controller syntax, with WebAPI responding to controllers actions that are named like HTTP Verbs. In the next version of ASP.NET, this mix-and-match scenario goes away. There will be one framework that provides this Controller-centric server-side capabilities. All of these capabiltes are housed in the new Microsoft.AspNet.Mvc framework.</p> <h2>Bye Bye System.Web</h2> <p>Alas after 15 years, we say goodbye to System.Web. We knew you so well&hellip;.</p> <p>To some, this is a welcome departure as the System.Web assembly is the second largest assembly in the base class library (BCL). This 5 megabyte monster was very difficult for the ASP.NET team to manage, and any changes to it required an update to the .NET BCL. Those changes are not deployed easily, and typically only ship with a new Visual Studio or a new version of Windows. In order to keep the ASP.NET run-time more nimble and adapting at the speed of the Internet, the ASP.NET team decided to move all new development out of System.Web.</p> <p>This also means that ASP.NET web forms is not coming along for the ride into ASP.NET vNext. This does not mean that they will stop maintaining it. It does mean that the changes to it will not be as quick, and that you can still use Microsoft IIS to host your web forms application. None of that has changed or will change for a very long time.</p> <h2>Blistering Performance</h2> <p>The demonstrations on stage at TechEd were nothing short of impressive. Granted, the demos that are currently being shown are very small. The compile and execution time of the code is amazing. In fact, you don&rsquo;t have to compile at all. The Roslyn compiler will automatically make those changes and deploy them for you when the code files change!</p> <h2>Where is web.config?</h2> <p>Configuration files? Who needs configuration files! The .config file architecture was an artifact of the System.Web library. At this point, why write a configuration file when you can hard-code your configuration in C# code and Roslyn will reload it faster than you can say &ldquo;I saved a change to the configuration.&rdquo; Look for a new configuration standard to come along soon, and it will probably be based on JSON, given the new project.json file structure. Which brings us to&hellip;</p> <h2>Welcome Project.JSON!</h2> <p>No more XML .csproj nonsense with build artifacts and configuration defined. In this next version of ASP.NET, we will have a JSON file that lists our dependencies, the configurations we want the application to run in, and the commands that we can use against the web application.</p> <p>Dependencies are kicked up a whole new level, as they no longer require a compiled DLL to reside on disk. You can now add references to NuGet packages, and ASP.NET will fetch that dependency when you first deploy and run your application. This allows you to compose your application from more NuGet building blocks, and adhere to those <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_%28object-oriented_design">SOLID principles</a>).</p> <p>Project.json has a syntax structure similar to the following:</p> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">{</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"version"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: </code><code style="color: blue;">"0.1"</code><code style="color: #000000;">,</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"dependencies"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: {</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">},</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"commands"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: {</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"run"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: </code><code style="color: blue;">""</code><code style="color: #000000;">, </code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"web"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: </code><code style="color: blue;">""</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">},</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"configurations"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: {</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"net45"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: {}</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: blue;">"k10"</code><code style="color: #000000;">: {} </code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 6px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></div> </div> <br /> <p>You can force your project to download and run with the NuGet packages specified in your project.json file with this syntax:</p> <pre><code>kpm restore </code></pre> <h2>What does this mean for Telerik UI Customers?</h2> <p>At this time, we are eagerly watching the evolution of ASP.NET, and think this is going to be an amazing product for our customers. We will make whatever changes we need in order to support our <a href="http://www.telerik.com/aspnet-mvc">MVC helpers</a> in this new ASP.NET. Additionally, we will continue to support and add cool, modern and mobile functionality to our <a href="http://www.telerik.com/products/aspnet-ajax.aspx">UI for ASP.NET</a> controls.</p> <p>You can continue to build server-side APIs and deliver single-page-application structures that host the KendoUI JavaScript framework, even in this preview build from Microsoft.</p> <h2>Summary</h2> <p>At Telerik, we are very excited about the changes being introduced by Microsoft in this next generation of ASP.NET. We will be there for you as this project from Microsoft moves towards completion. Its all part of Telerik&rsquo;s commitment to support any software development in any development environment and targeting any screen.</p> <br />
Building an Appointment Tracking App by using Teleriks WP Cloud Components - Part 1|Cloud|<p>Hello everyone,</p> <p>With the recent release of our cloud-powered controls for Windows Phone 8, now is a great time to begin using them. Using these components, they can help you create an application that stores its data in the cloud that can be easily accessed by multiple users on multiple devices. In this blog post, I will walk you through several of the cloud-powered controls currently available and teach you how to use them in your app. We&rsquo;ll create an app called &ldquo;AppointmentTracker&rdquo;, which allows users to store information about upcoming appointments and so forth.</p> <h4>Getting Setup</h4> <p>Before we can get started, make sure that you have the&nbsp;<a href="http://www.telerik.com/products/windows-phone/download.aspx">latest bits</a>&nbsp;installed. The Q3 2013 release contains both our traditional components as well as our cloud components.</p> <p>After you have installed our controls, you will find several new templates. The one we are interested in working with today is called &ldquo;Telerik Cloud Powered Windows Phone App&rdquo; as shown in&nbsp;<strong>Figure 1. <br /> </strong></p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]7efaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]7efaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="1_thumb2" alt="1_thumb2" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]7efaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p><strong>Figure 1:</strong>&nbsp;The new cloud template installed with RadControls for Windows Phone 8.</p> <p>The &ldquo;<strong>Telerik Cloud Powered Windows Phone App</strong>&rdquo; template is where we are going to begin, so select it and give it a name and press OK.</p> <p>After doing so, you will be presented with a Cloud Services Configuration dialog, where you will need to enter your username and password and accept the terms and conditions. From this screen you can either select an existing project or create a new one. Let&rsquo;s create a new one as shown in&nbsp;<strong>Figure 2</strong>. This information will carry over to our Everlive account which we will take a look at shortly.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]8afaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]8afaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="2_thumb2" alt="2_thumb2" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]8afaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <p><strong>Figure 2:</strong>&nbsp;Creating a new project with our Cloud Controls.</p> <p>From here, we can simply click the &ldquo;Create&rdquo; button, then finish and our project will be loaded in Visual Studio 2012 or 2013.</p> <h4>Changing the Configuration Type</h4> <p>Launch the Configuration Manager and change the &ldquo;Active Solution Platform&rdquo; to&nbsp;<strong>x86</strong>&nbsp;instead of&nbsp;<strong>ARM</strong>, where we can test this on an emulator instead of a device as shown in&nbsp;<strong>Figure 3</strong>.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]96faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]96faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="3_thumb2547564359A85" alt="3_thumb2547564359A85" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]96faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <p><strong>Figure 3:</strong>&nbsp;Changing the Configuration Manager.</p> <h4>The Appointment Tracker App</h4> <p>Now that the app is created and connected to the cloud service, let&rsquo;s add three Windows Phone Portrait pages. Now I have my ClientId and ClientSecret.<br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]abfaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]abfaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="8_thumb-1-99DAD26CA5B5" alt="8_thumb-1-99DAD26CA5B5" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]abfaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br />If we look at our designer, we should have the following screen as shown in&nbsp;<strong>Figure 4</strong>.<br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]b7faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]b7faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="4_thumb-3-39EA092472EF" alt="4_thumb-3-39EA092472EF" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]b7faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p><strong>Figure 4:</strong>&nbsp;RadCloudLogin with the various social providers.</p> <p><em>Please note that Google and LiveID you will need to setup an app similar to the one we did for Facebook.</em></p> <p>Let&rsquo;s switch over to everlive.com and log into our AppointmentTracker instance and go to settings, then user authentication. Make sure you have a check mark in Facebook, Google and Windows Live as shown in&nbsp;<strong>Figure 5</strong>. This will give our app more flexibility in the future if we chose to add Google and the the Live ID providers.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]c3faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]c3faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="5_thumb-3-28401BFC05F8" alt="5_thumb-3-28401BFC05F8" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]c3faff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p><strong>Figure 5:</strong>&nbsp;Adding Everlive permissions to the various social providers.</p> <p>If we run our app with a valid Client ID and Client Secret for Facebook, we would see the following screen after the user touches the Facebook provider as shown in&nbsp;<strong>Figure 6</strong>.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]cffaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]cffaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="6_thumb-4-C0050F3B82C1" alt="6_thumb-4-C0050F3B82C1" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]cffaff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p><strong>Figure 6:</strong>&nbsp;Using Facebook instead of creating another username and password.</p> <p>As you can see we can now log in using Facebook and after a successful login, it will take us to our stubbed out &ldquo;Appointment.xaml&rdquo; All of this is coming in part 2, so stay tuned!</p> <p>Thanks,</p>Michael Crump<br /> <a href="https://twitter.com/@mbcrump">@mbcrump</a><br /> <br /> <br /> <br /> <br />

Windows Notifications Demystified|.NET|<p>Users only spend a limited amount of time in our apps; anything we developers can do to invite them back into our apps increases user engagement. One of the best tools for doing this in Windows Store apps are notifications such as toasts, tiles and badges. We can trigger timely and appropriate notifications from outside (mostly from a cloud service) for Windows Store app users, even when our app is not actively running. The goal for using things like live tiles, badge updates and toast notifications is simple: keep the user feeling connected and engaged to your app. In this article, we take a closer look at Windows Store app notifications and Push Notification architecture, laying the groundwork before we look at how to trigger such Push Notifications from cloud services. Let&rsquo;s dive in.</p> <h2>Notification Types</h2> <h3>Tiles</h3> <p>App&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh779724.aspx">Tiles</a>&nbsp;are the front doors to your Windows Store apps, and your first opportunity to make a good impression with the user. Tiles are the representations of your app on the Windows Start screen and, most commonly, the launch pad of how the user starts your app. So, a well-branded app tile with pertinent information invites the user into your app. Tiles come in several sizes, and can display text, images, app branding and notification badges. A sample assortment of tile types is as below.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]07fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img title="Tiles-1024x511" alt="Tiles-1024x511" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]07fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></span><br /> <br />The anatomy of a tile is based on a non-extensible set of Windows-provided XML templates, as defined in the&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh761491.aspx">Tile Template catalog</a>. This is an exhaustive list where each template provides an unique way to present a mix of text and images in varying sizes, based on how the user may have the app tile configured on their start screen. Tile templates can be static or peek; the contents of a peek tile scrolls up and down within the tile space to display the full communication, while static tiles are fixed. A quick sample of a named Tile template, along with the XML needed to construct the tile is shown below.<br /> <br /> <p>As we talk Push Notifications later, let&rsquo;s remember the Tile Template catalog &ndash; a large assortment of Tile notifications that can be used to entice the user back into our app. Also, tile notifications can be cycled, automatically rotating through up to five current notifications on the tile, using&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh781199.aspx">notification queues</a>.</p> <h3>Secondary Tiles</h3> <p>If the user experience of diving directly to a certain section of the app is beneficial, developers have the option of offering the ability to pin a separate tile on the start screen, called the&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh465372.aspx">Secondary Tile</a>. Each Windows Store app decides which content to offer for pinning, but the user chooses whether the secondary tile will be created or deleted; this cannot be controlled programmatically. Secondary tiles allow the user to personalize their start screen with the experiences they use the most, like specific cities for a Weather app or catalogs in a News app. Secondary tiles are different from the main app tile and can receive notification updates independently, still adhering to the Tile Template catalog guidelines. When the user launches an app from a Secondary tile, an activation context or deep link is provided, so that developers get to direct the user experience to a specific part of the app.</p> <h3>Badges</h3> <p>Badges often convey summary or status information specific to your app. They appear on top of the live tiles and may often be confused as a part of the tile itself. Badges, however, stand on their own with matching&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/br212849.aspx">schema templates</a>&nbsp;and can be updated independent of the app tile. Badges show up usually in the right bottom corner of the tiles and can often be the reason users tap/click on a tile.</p> <p>A badge can convey either a numeric value from 1-99 or a Windows defined glyph. The numbers are often used to provide information that can be counted, like the number of unread messages in an email app, for instance. The glyphs are non-extensible and provide summary information like social media availability or media playback controls. Two sample badge updates, one with numeric value and the other with a glyph, are shown below.</p>The available glyphs for use with Windows are shown below, along with their XML equivalent in the badge template.<br /> <h3>Toasts</h3> <p>A&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh779727.aspx">Toast notification</a>&nbsp;is a transient message shown to the user that contains relevant, time-sensitive information and provides quick access to related content in the parent app. It is like an invitation back into your app. Now, unlike tiles or badges that only show up in the Start screen, a transient toast notification shows up in the top right corner (or left based on the globalization settings) of the user&rsquo;s screen, irrespective of what the user is up to anywhere in Windows. So, toast notifications can be quite powerful to draw attention, but this calls for restraint as the user has the option of enabling/disabling toasts at any time.</p> <p>An sample assortment of possible toast notifications is shown below.</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]13fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]13fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="Toasts" alt="Toasts" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]13fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br />Just like tiles, toasts can contain a combination of text and/or images, as per the pre-defined anatomy listed in the<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh761494.aspx">Toast Template catalog</a>. A sample toast notification along with the XML template is shown below.<br /> <br /> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]1ffdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]1ffdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="ToastTemplateExample-1024x577" alt="ToastTemplateExample-1024x577" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]1ffdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <p>Toasts can optionally be of longer transient duration, be scheduled at specific and recurring times or&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/hh761492.aspx">play sounds</a>upon arrival. In short, toasts are a very powerful notification mechanism in Windows that immediately attracts the user&rsquo;s attention, with an invite back into the parent app.</p> <h2>Notification Delivery</h2> <p>Now that we talked about all the different notification types supported in Windows Stores apps &ndash; namely tiles, badges and toasts, how does one fire up these notifications? Let&rsquo;s look at options we have as developers to deliver such notifications to our app users. There are four:</p> <ol> <li><strong>Local</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; local updates necessitate that the Windows app is being run by the user, and generates a notification update from inside the app. There are well-defined APIs in Windows that allow developers to send out such notifications while the app is running. This works for all of&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/Hh868253(v=win.10).aspx">tile</a>,&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/Hh868254(v=win.10).aspx">toast</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/Hh868225(v=win.10).aspx">badge</a>&nbsp;notifications.</li> <li><strong>Scheduled</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; scheduled updates are similar to local, except that the notification delivery is scheduled in future at a precise date and time. This works for&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/Hh868223(v=win.10).aspx">tile</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/Hh868224(v=win.10).aspx">toast</a>&nbsp;notifications.</li> <li><strong>Periodic</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; periodic updates set up a&nbsp;<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/windows/apps/xaml/jj150587.aspx">periodic schedule</a>&nbsp;where an external URL source (website or cloud service) is polled at defined intervals to pull down the latest content for notification delivery. This works for tiles and badges.</li> <li><strong>Push</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; This is the truest form of external content/notification delivery mechanism, since nothing is initialized from the client Windows app. It involves your service, performing a handshake with the&nbsp;<em>Windows Push Notification Service</em>&nbsp;(WNS) to route notification messages to subscribing clients. This delivery mechanism can happen at any time (ideal for real-time scenarios), and works with tile, badge or toast notifications. We&rsquo;ll take a deeper look at Push Notifications next.</li> </ol> <h3>Push Notification Architecture</h3> <p>There are couple of steps involved for developers to enable push notifications in their apps. The Push Notification architecture is represented in the following figure:</p> <span class="sfImageWrapper" data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]2bfdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><a href="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]2bfdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b"><img openoriginalimageonclick="true" title="PushArchitecture" alt="PushArchitecture" displaymode="Original" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]2bfdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" /></a></span><br /> <br /> <p>My good friend&nbsp;<a href="https://twitter.com/jeffblankenburg">Jeff Blankenburg</a>&nbsp;uses the following real-world scenario to describe how the push notifications architecture works:</p> <blockquote> <p>Say you got a new house. What&rsquo;s the first thing you do? You head over to the Post Office to get yourself an address. Next, what good is you knowing the address of your own home? So, you immediately inform your family/friends of your new home address &ndash; may be even ask for house-warming gifts. Now, your family/friends acknowledge your new address; but when they need to send you a package, they do not often swing by your house in person. Instead, they normally send the packages to the Post Office asking for delivery to your house address, which in turn, sends out postmen to deliver the packages.</p> </blockquote> <p>This is very similar to what goes on to empower Windows Store apps with push notifications from the cloud. Here&rsquo;s a breakdown of the steps involved (the step numbers relate to the figure above):</p> <ul> <li><strong>Steps 1,2,3</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; Your Windows Store app needs to get a unique identifier (the home address) for the device it is running on. The app requests for a unique identifier from the Windows Notification Client Platform, which conveys the message to the Windows Notification Service (WNS) asking for a Notification Channel. The WNS is the Post Office in this story. Upon request, the WNS generates a unique URI as the Notification Channel that identifies the given Windows app on the user&rsquo;s given device and returns the URI to the caller.</li> <li><strong>Step 4</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; Next, a Cloud service (family/friends in our story) comes into the play, which is the backend companion to your Windows app. While there are Backend-as-a-Service offerings, this really can be any service that is internet facing. This service however, needs to know about the unique Notification Channel URI for each app subscriber, so it knows where to send the push notifications.</li> <li>Next, the cloud service needs to do a little handshake (per OAuth 2 protocol) with WNS to authenticate itself. Our Windows app is obviously registered with the Windows Store, thus receiving a Package Security Identifier (SID) and a Client Secret key. &nbsp;This needs to be shared with the cloud service, so that it can identify itself while talking to WNS. In return, WNS provides the cloud service with an Access Token, which is to be included in all future communication until the token expires.</li> <li><strong>Step 5</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; When the cloud service has something important to share with the client Windows Store app, it sends a request to WNS including the payload of the message. What does a notification payload look like? Yep, this is where we go back to the Tile/Toast/Badge template catalogs and choose what&rsquo;s appropriate. This request to WNS requires authentication through the access token provided before and is in the form of an HTTP Post request at the unique client Channel URI.</li> <li><strong>Step 6</strong>&nbsp;&ndash; WNS receives the HTTP request containing the notification payload and routes it to the appropriate Windows client device.</li> </ul> <h2>Conclusion</h2> <p>In this article, we reviewed the various notification types and delivery mechanisms for keeping your Windows Store app users engaged. We also took a closer look at the architectural pieces behind getting Push Notifications to work for Windows users. Overall, there are lots of choices for developers to keep their users feeling connected to their app at all times. With the stage set, I&rsquo;ll cover more details on implementing push notifications from the cloud in upcoming articles.</p> <p>Also, if you are building Windows 8.1 apps in HTML or XAML, Telerik UI for Windows 8 offers a robust toolset of controls and framework pieces. Give the free trial a spin today!</p> <em>Note: Many of the images used in this article are courtesy of Microsoft.</em><br /> <br />
2014 Buyer's Guide: Select the Best Testing Tool for Your Project|Testing|<p>Choosing the right test automation tool for your project can be a gruesome process. There&rsquo;s a vast array of tools available in the market, from small single-purpose executable applications to complex systems that require their own significant infrastructure. When selecting a test automation solution, you&rsquo;ll need to consider many factors. You&rsquo;ll want to measure each solution against your&nbsp;<strong>current and future needs</strong>&nbsp;and you&rsquo;ll need to carefully weigh these factors against&nbsp;<strong>open and hidden costs</strong>. Knowing where to start, and what your milestones should be, can help you save budget, time and frustration in the long run.</p> <p>First off, before you dive into tool comparisons and lengthy feature checklists, take the time to understand the automation needs of your organization. Be sure to ask yourself:</p> <ul> <li>What issues are we trying to solve?</li> <li>Who will be the users and what&rsquo;s their skillset?</li> <li>How does it add business value?</li> </ul> <p>Once you know the answers, the&nbsp;<a href="http://www.telerik.com/campaigns/teststudio/qa-test-automation-buyers-guide">2014 Buyer&rsquo;s Guide for Test Automation Tools</a>&nbsp;by industry expert Jim Holmes will help you outline the most important considerations you need to have in mind when at the beginning of a testing tool selection process.</p> <p><a href="http://www.telerik.com/campaigns/teststudio/qa-test-automation-buyers-guide"><span data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]6dfdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" class="sfImageWrapper"><img width="500" height="400" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]6dfdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" alt="buyersguide-blog-image[1]" displaymode="Original" title="buyersguide-blog-image[1]" style="margin: 10px; Why Should Developers Help With User Interface Tests?|Testing|<p>Automated User Interface (UI) testing has a deserved reputation for being a morass of low-value, high-cost pain. Developers are already over-burdened trying to get the work in front of them completed and into the delivery pipeline. Why should they look to take on additional work in helping create functional tests at the User Interface level?</p> <h2>Ship Better Value</h2> <br /> <p>Every developer should care about delivering better value to their customers, regardless whether their customers are internal or external. Better value means the delivered software is in alignment with what those customers true needs are (often not what they&nbsp;<em>asked&nbsp;</em>for). Better value means software that is performant enough in the customer's environment to keep the customer's users happy. Better value means few, if any bugs escaped out to the customer requiring support and patches.</p> <p>Important sounding stuff, no? And these are just a few of the reasons why developers ought to take interest and have a stake in UI-level testing.</p> <p><span style="line-height: 1.2; font-size: 18px;">Test All the Scary Things</span></p> <p>UI testing is time consuming, both for the initial creation and ongoing maintenance. With that in mind, teams that are already busy building the software can't afford to disrupt their current flow for low-value, senseless automation.</p> <p>Instead, teams should focus on the most critical, high value features. What's keeping your stakeholders awake at night? Generally it's around two broad areas: Can we make money? and Are we going to get sued?</p> <p>Flippant prose aside, these really are two solid areas to focus on: are the features that impact revenue (or your business-critical work) stable and working? Are you properly securing highly sensitive data? Of course you'd use those as a starting base; the point being: talk with your stakeholders and understand the most critical areas of the system.</p> <p>Take a shopping cart system as an example. Simple testing of the checkout system UI isn&rsquo;t enough. You&rsquo;d want to ensure your integrations with third-party payment providers such as PayPal, Discover, and Visa was rock-solid. If integrations with those providers aren&rsquo;t working properly then your company is likely losing revenue and incurring extra customer service costs due to failed transactions.</p> <p><strong><em>That's</em></strong><em>&nbsp;</em>the place where you begin building your UI scripts, not automating checks around look and feel or icon placement!</p> <p><span style="line-height: 1.2; font-size: 18px;">How Much to Test?</span></p> <p>UI tests should be viewed as an extension and companion to other types of testing you are (or should be) doing already. A well-tested system will have a mix of different test types. Lisa Crispin and Janet Gregory use a "testing pyramid" as an example in their book&nbsp;<a href="http://www.amazon.com/Agile-Testing-Practical-Guide-Testers/dp/0321534468/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1400267262&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=lisa+crispin" target="_blank">Agile Testing</a>.</p> <p>Unit tests--those with no dependencies--should make up the majority of automated tests in a system because they're fast to write and run, and maintenance costs are lowest. The middle of the pyramid is integration tests--those crossing service boundaries--since they're slower to run and costlier to write and maintain. UI tests with their high costs should be at the top of the pyramid: a few carefully chosen tests that exercise high-value flows.</p> <p>Let&rsquo;s take some examples from computing wages as part of a payroll system. Here&rsquo;s how things might break out:</p> <ul> <li><strong>Unit Tests</strong>: Test the method/algorithm calculating wages. Validate proper wage is computed for workers based on inputs of hours worked, worker&rsquo;s hourly rate, and whether or not the worker earns overtime.</li> <li><strong>Integration Tests</strong>: Test interaction to other systems/layers. Check the security system integration to ensure only permitted users can execute payroll activities.</li> <li><strong>UI Tests</strong>: Check the flow of generating payroll. Validate an authorized user can click the right buttons and see that the payroll was properly executed.</li> </ul> <p>These last paragraphs centered on automated tests. You should be having some form of manual testing going on as well! I won't get into the debates around the various schools of testing. Suffice it to say you should have skilled testers exploring through your system alongside your automated tests.</p> <p><span style="line-height: 1.2; font-size: 18px;">Does It Really Work Like You Thought?</span></p> <p>Interactions across boundaries are a frequent source of bugs. It's not surprising because invoking those calls, managing their lifecycle, and handling errors/exceptions is a relatively difficult task. Integration tests help drive great design and solid API behavior across those boundaries, but it's not enough to stop there.</p> <p>Testing a workflow from the UI all the way down to the database is the final step in ensuring your systems' interactions are behaving properly. Is your UI not updating properly after a CRUD action? An integration or unit test wouldn't have caught that. Is caching interfering with rows in your displayed grid updating? Whoops! Discovering that's the domain of a functional UI test too.</p> <h2>Automation as Acceptance Tests</h2> <br /> <p>Automation frameworks can also serve as acceptance criteria for new work, regardless of whether you're using some form of specification toolset (See Gojko Adzik's wonderful book&nbsp;<a href="http://www.amazon.com/Specification-Example-Successful-Deliver-Software/dp/1617290084/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1400267855&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=specification+by+example" target="_blank">Specification By Example</a>) or just tying your automation to acceptance criteria in&nbsp;<a href="http://www.telerik.com/teampulse" target="_blank">Telerik TeamPulse</a>.</p> <p>Cucumber is one popular tool that lets teams create text specifications over the top of drivers like Telerik Testing Framework or WebDriver. Cucumber lets you write things like:</p> <p> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">Given I am logged on </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">as</code> <code style="color: #000000;">a payroll administrator</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">When I edit an employee&rsquo;s pay rate</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">Then I am able to update their salary</code></span></div> </div> </p> <p><span style="line-height: 1.5;">Below is a similar scenario using an automated test in Telerik Test Studio tied to acceptance criteria in TeamPulse.</span></p> <p> <br /> <span data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]87fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" class="sfImageWrapper"><img width="970" height="480" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]87fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" alt="tp_ts_criteria image" displaymode="Original" title="tp_ts_criteria_970[1]" style="margin: 10px; border-width: 0px; border-style: solid;" /></span></p> <p>Using automation in this fashion helps you ensure what you're building is as close as possible to what the customer's actually looking for. You're gaining not only a regression safety net, you're also gaining confidence the system's functioning as your customers expect. You'll also gain a bit of living documentation if you're taking care in how you write your acceptance criteria.</p> <h2>UI Tests? Why Me? Let The Testers Do That!</h2> <br /> <p>Too often UI tests are seen in the domain of the "test team." Long years of hard knocks and struggling (or outright failing) automation efforts have finally started to bring about some much-needed change in how and where UI tests are being created. Successful teams are bringing testing and development activities and roles much closer together--and many teams are outright mixing them totally in "whole team testing" delivery flows.</p> <p>Even if developers don't create the UI tests themselves, they're in a great spot to help the automated suites be extremely successful.</p> <p>Developers can make the UI much more testable by scattering good IDs across critical elements. They can also help UI tests deal with tricky asynchronous situations by providing appropriate latches when async calls have completed. Backing APIs to help leverage system functionality for test setup and teardown? Devs are all over that.</p> <p>As an example, the following snippet of code will update the ID row in a Kendo UI Grid to be based on the ContactName text from a record:</p> <p> <div class="reCodeBlock" style="border: 1px solid #7f9db9; overflow-y: auto;"> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">dataBound: </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">function</code> <code style="color: #000000;">(dataBoundEvent) {</code></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">gridWidget = dataBoundEvent.sender;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">dataSource = gridWidget.dataSource;</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">$.each(gridWidget.items(), </code><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">function</code> <code style="color: #000000;">(index, item) {</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">uid = $(item).data(</code><code style="color: blue;">"uid"</code><code style="color: #000000;">);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px ! important;"><code style="color: #006699; font-weight: bold;">var</code> <code style="color: #000000;">dataItem = dataSource.getByUid(uid);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 18px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">$(item).attr(</code><code style="color: blue;">"id"</code><code style="color: #000000;">, dataItem.ContactName);</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span><code>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</code><span style="margin-left: 12px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">});</code></span></span></div> <div style="background-color: #f8f8f8;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;">&nbsp;</span></div> <div style="background-color: #ffffff;"><span style="margin-left: 0px ! important;"><code style="color: #000000;">}</code></span></div> </div> </p> <h2>Bring on the Geek: Performance and Load Testing</h2> <p>For devs who feel general UI automation isn't cool enough, think about a couple seriously geeky types of testing: Performance and Load. UI testing is the gateway to seeing just how well your system holds up in the real environment. Get a few UI tests in place, and it's an easy step to begin working with CPU utilization rates, cache hits, disk bottlenecks, and time to first byte metrics.</p> <p><span style="line-height: 1.2; font-size: 18px;">Bringing It Home: Get Testing!</span></p> <p>Developers can bring great value to their organizations by learning the fundamentals of UI automation. Even if developers don't do the majority of the UI automation, understanding how the process and tools work means devs can ensure the system is as testable as possible. Devs can lend a hand writing the automation to ensure it's as robust as possible, and they can utilize their system knowledge to help flesh out critical aspects of the test infrastructure.</p> <p>Tools like&nbsp;<a href="http://www.telerik.com/teststudio/visual-studio-testing-plugin-benefits">Telerik Test Studio's plugin for Visual Studio</a>&nbsp;make it simple for developers to quickly help adding value to their automated test suites. Developers can either quickly add value to automation written by testers, or they can quickly build stable, flexible automated tests themselves. Moreover, developers will see great additional features if they&rsquo;re also using Telerik UI controls. Wrappers for many Telerik controls provide additional functionality to instantly verify things like row counts in Rad or Kendo UI grids, check the count of nodes in a KendoTreeView, or deal with complex scope changes of elements on the page.<br /> <br /> <span data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]92fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" class="sfImageWrapper"><img width="644" height="294" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]92fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" alt="grid_xlator_narrow" displaymode="Original" title="grid_xlator_narrow[2]" style="border-width: 0px; border-style: solid;" /></span><strong><br />Figure 1: Working with different element scopes in a RadGrid&nbsp;</strong></p> <p><span data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]9efdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" class="sfImageWrapper"><img width="612" height="403" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]9efdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" alt="tree_xlator" style="line-height: 1.5; border-width: 0px; border-style: solid;" displaymode="Original" title="tree_xlator" /></span></p> <p> <strong>Figure 2: Quick validation of node count in a Kendo UI TreeView</strong></p> <p>Have we gotten your interest yet? The next posts in this short series will dive into specifics for some of Telerik&rsquo;s popular controls. You&rsquo;ll be seeing more details around Kendo UI and AJAX controls!</p> <p>Are you finding UI testing useful in your organizations? How are you working together with your testers? Let us know in the comments!</p>

Introducing Telerik Mobile Testing|Testing|<p>Telerik Mobile Testing is an automated functional testing solution developed at Telerik. It allows developers to write automated functional tests in JavaScript using a set of APIs we devised to make the process as easy as possible. The environment in which these tests are executed is node.js. If you aren't yet familiar with node.js, I highly recommend&nbsp;<a href="http://nodejs.org/">checking it out</a>, as I guarantee you will want to use it in one of your own projects.<br /> <br />Basically, node.js is a platform built on the V8 JavaScript Engine. It provides APIs that make it incredibly easy to spin up different types of servers, utilities, etc. In addition to this, since it's based on JavaScript you can run it on each of the big three operating systems, Windows, OS X, and Linux. This is incredibly important in relation to mobile development, because developers need quick access to tools on the operating systems they either choose, or are restricted to during development.<br /> <br />We are leveraging the power of node.js in Telerik Mobile Testing to create a web sockets relay server. This server acts as a bridge between our Test Running tools, and mobile devices. In adopting this architecture, we've afforded ourselves the ability to easily add support for automating new platforms, and the ability to provide a consistent set of automation APIs across platforms.<br /> <br />For this initial release, we have provided support for the following platforms.</p> <ul style="list-style-type: disc;"> <li>iOS</li> <li>Android</li> </ul> <p>Note that we don't consider this initial support for these platforms THE support for these platforms. We have long term plans to expand this support to the best of our abilities. In the short term we will be adding more support for automating specific controls, and even for performing gestures. In the long term, we plan to adapt our platform support to whatever Apple, Google, or whoever else throw at us.</p> <h2>Getting Started</h2> <p>Telerik Mobile Testing is part of the incredible new&nbsp;<a href="http://www.telerik.com/platform">Telerik Platform</a>. Don't worry, this isn't the point where you have to pull out your credit card. The Telerik Platform Starter Edition is&nbsp;<strong>FREE</strong>, and guess what. The full version of Telerik Mobile Testing is included with the starter edition! So, please, go check out the platform, and everything it has to offer. Once you've finished, come back here and we can continue.<br /> <br />...<br /> <br />Welcome back! So, now that you've got your Telerik Platform account all set up, let's talk about where you need to go to get Telerik Mobile Testing. In the top right of the Telerik Platform Interface, there is an icon with an arrow pointing down at a rectangle. Clicking on this icon will take you to the downloads page.</p> <p><span data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d4fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" class="sfImageWrapper"><img width="210" height="156" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]d4fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" alt="platformdownloadspage" displaymode="Original" title="platformdownloadspage[1]" style="border-width: 0px; border-style: solid;" /></span><br /> <em>The downloads link</em></p> <p>&nbsp;</p>Once on the downloads page, scroll to the section titled "Download SDKs &amp; Tools". Once there, click on the "Mobile Testing" rectangle. This will take you to the Telerik Mobile Testing downloads page. On this page, you will see a link to download the Mobile Testing Framework, and links for the iOS, and Android companion apps.<br /> <br /> <h2>Mobile Testing Framework</h2> <br />This is the core tooling needed for the mobile testing framework. It includes the following.<br /> <br /> <br /> <ul style="list-style-type: disc;"> <li>JavaScript automated testing framework</li> <li>Automation extensions for each supported platform</li> <li>Command line test runner</li> <li>GUI test runner</li> <li>Demo Applications for each supported platform w/sample tests</li> <li>Companion apps for simulators and emulators</li> </ul>Installing the framework is as easy as following&nbsp;<a href="http://docs.telerik.com/platform/mobile-testing/installation">this step-by-step guide</a>&nbsp;in our documentation.<br /> <br /> <h3>Companion app for iOS</h3> <br />The iOS companion app, available on the App Store, acts as a gateway to performing testing of Native and Hybrid iOS apps. You simply establish a connection between it, and the relay server provided in the Mobile Testing Framework. Once you've deployed a debug version of your app built with the iOS automation extension, you can launch it and automate it via a JavaScript test executed in one of the test runners.<br /> <br /> <h3>Companion app for Android</h3> <br />The Android companion app is also available in the App Store. Like the iOS companion app, it acts as the gateway to performing testing of Native and Hybrid Android apps. You simply connect it to the relay server provided in the Mobile Testing Framework download, and use it to launch Android apps that have been extended with our automation extension for testing.<br /> <br /> <br /> <h2>Documentation</h2> <p>Once you've downloaded all of the above tools, you can refer to our&nbsp;<a href="http://docs.telerik.com/platform/mobile-testing/overview">documentation</a>&nbsp;for information about everything you need to know regarding mobile testing. A few key topics I recommend reading include the following.</p> <ul style="list-style-type: disc;"> <li><a href="http://docs.telerik.com/platform/mobile-testing/installation">Installation</a></li> <li>Demos</li> </ul> <p>&nbsp;</p> <h3>Telerik Platform Integration</h3> <p>Now that you've gotten your hands dirty, you may be asking yourself, how does this fit into the Telerik Platform? Well, I'm glad you asked! You'll be happy to know that by using the Telerik Platform, you can track the long term health of your application in a single place. You can see how many tests passed or failed over the past month. In addition to this, if there is a test failure, you can drill into it by clicking on it to learn more about why the failure occurred.</p> <p><span data-sfref="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e0fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" class="sfImageWrapper"><img width="500" height="371" src="[images|OpenAccessDataProvider]e0fdff31-ebfb-67f3-ba7d-ff000072e94b" alt="mobiletestingplatform[1]" displaymode="Original" title="mobiletestingplatform[1]" style="margin: 10px; border-width: 0px; border-style: solid;" /></span></p> <p><em>Mobile Testing Results in the Telerik Platform</em></p> <p>Uploading your test results to the Telerik Platform is as easy as setting an API Key in the GUI or CLI test runner tools. You can retrieve your API Key by clicking the "API Key" link at the top of the test results page.<br /> <br />...</p> <p>&nbsp;<br />I hope you've enjoyed learning about some of what Telerik Mobile Testing has to offer.
